# TEMPERATURE GRADIENTS IN THERMAL SLEEVES

The fuel tubes pass through the pressure shell heads in the manner shown schematically in Fig. 23a. The sleeve connecting the pressure shell and fuel tube is at the pressure shell temperature at one end and at the fuel tube temperature at the other end. At the fuel tube outlet, the fuel tubes are at $1500^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ ; the pressure shell temperature at this point is difficult to determine, but it is probably not less than $1150^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . The sleeve gains heat from the fuel tube through the NaK between the tube and sleeve; the NaK is assumed to be stagnant. Heat lost by the sleeve to the surrounding helium atmosphere may be neglected because the helium is at about $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . The problem was simplified by considering the sleeve, the fuel tube, and the NaK between the sleeve and the fuel tube as a single cylindrical tube. The temperature was assumed to be constant across any cross section of this cylindrical tube, and an average thermal conductivity for the tube was used. (The conductivities of NaK and Inconel are nearly equal.) The differential equation governing the flow of heat in the sleeve is (Fig. 23b)

$$
- k a \frac {d ^ {2} T}{d x ^ {2}} = h c (1 5 0 0 - T), \tag {93}
$$

where

$k =$ thermal conductivity of the composite tube, Btu/hr·ft² (°F/ft),

$a =$ cross-sectional area of the composite tube, ft²,

![](images/0b034594bbdbf85bc0a3e14ee2b0cc7db8498ec3cc20f99d49993e774d77e61a.jpg)

![](images/86861300ff57bfdeff8803e4c300f370fa1db9f70a89b8f0a023b478e996d5e2.jpg)  
Fig. 23. Thermal Sleeve.

$T =$ temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{F}$

$x =$ distance from pressure shell, ft,

$h =$ fuel heat transfer coefficient, Btu/hr·ft²·°F,

$c =$ circumference of inside of fuel tube, ft.

The boundary conditions are

(94) $T = 1150^{\circ} \mathrm{F}, \quad \text{at} x = 0$ ,   
(95) $T = 1500^{\circ}\mathrm{F},$ at $\pmb {x} = 0.1146$

The fuel heat transfer coefficient may be evaluated by using the following formula:

$$
h = 0. 0 2 7 \frac {k}{D} \operatorname {R e} ^ {0. 8} \Pr^ {1 / 3} \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) ^ {0. 1 4}, \tag {96}
$$

where

$k =$ thermal conductivity of the fuel, Btu/hr·ft² (°F/ft),

$D =$ fuel tube inner diameter, ft,

Re = Reynolds' number for the fuel,

$\mathbf{Pr} = \mathbf{Prandtl}$ 's number for the fuel,

$\frac{\mu}{\mu_s} = \text{ratio of fuel viscosity at bulk temperature to fuel viscosity at surface temperature.}$

For this case,

$$
\begin{array}{l} k = 1. 5 \mathrm {B t u} / \mathrm {h r} \cdot \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} \left(^ {\circ} \mathrm {F} / \mathrm {f t}\right), \\ D = 0. 0 9 2 9 f t, \\ \mathrm {R e} = 1 4, 1 0 0, \\ \Pr = 2. 8 5, \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) ^ {0. 1 4} = 0. 9 6 5 \\ h = 1 2 5 0 \mathrm {B t u / h r} \cdot \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} \cdot {} ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}. \\ \end{array}
$$

In Eq. 93,

$$
\begin{array}{l} k = 1 3. 7 \mathrm {B t u} / \mathrm {h r} \cdot \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} (^ {\circ} \mathrm {F} / \mathrm {f t}), \\ a = 0. 0 1 2 9 f t ^ {2}, \\ c = 0. 4 2 2 f t; \\ \end{array}
$$

therefore

$$
\frac {d ^ {2} T}{d x ^ {2}} - 2 9 8 0 T = - 4. 4 8 \times 1 0 ^ {6}. \tag {97}
$$

The solution of Eq. 97 is

$$
T = c _ {1} e ^ {5 4. 6 x} + c _ {2} e ^ {- 5 4. 6 x} + 1 5 0 0. \tag {98}
$$

The first boundary condition, Eq. 94, gives

$$
c _ {1} + c _ {2} = - 3 5 0. \tag {99}
$$

The second boundary condition, Eq. 95, gives

$$
5 2 0 c _ {1} + 0. 0 0 1 9 2 3 c _ {2} = 0. \tag {100}
$$

Therefore

$$
c _ {1} = 0. 0 0 1 2 9 4
$$

and

$$
c _ {2} = - 3 5 0.
$$

Thus Eq. 98 becomes

(101) $T = 0.001294 e^{54 \cdot 6x}$

$$
- 3 5 0 e ^ {- 5 4 \cdot 6 x} + 1 5 0 0,
$$

and the first derivative of $T$ with respect to $x$ is

(102) $\frac{dT}{dx} = 0.07065e^{54 \cdot 6x}$

$$
+ 1 9, 1 1 0 e ^ {- 5 4 \cdot 6 x}.
$$

Plots of Eqs. 101 and 102 are shown in Fig. 24. The maximum temperature gradient is about $1600^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ per inch.

Temperature in Fuel Tube Elbows as a Result of Afterheat of Residual Fuel. There is a possibility of fuel remaining in the U bends of the reactor fuel tubes after dumping. This residual fuel will rise in temperature because of internal heat generation, and it is desirable to know the maximum temperature that will be attained. Since the activity of the fuel decreases with time after shutdown, an equilibrium will be reached when the heat generation rate is the same as the rate of the heat loss. Once this equilibrium has been reached, the fuel temperature will decrease.

The total heat generation rate of the fuel is given in another portion of this report. From this, the heat generation rate of the fuel in a U bend is

$$
Q = \frac {\text {v o l u m e o f f u e l i n U b e n d}}{\text {t o t a l f u e l v o l u m e}} \times Q _ {f} ， \tag {103}
$$

where

$Q =$ heat generation rate, Btu/sec $Q_{f} =$ total heat generation rate of fuel, Btu/sec.

The temperature rise in the U bend will be greatest when the reflector coolant system is filled with helium (Fig. 9). Under this condition, heat is removed from the tube by free convection and radiation. The free

![](images/ce304a277884c65ede32c8a0cf55b5a650be67105ff751a764f1b05141eb16b2.jpg)

![](images/10dff7454818008f460b36c66b79c43fe4de475e23fbf3e1e7329dcaeab4f4ef.jpg)  
Fig. 24. Temperature and Temperature Gradient in Thermal Sleeve vs. Distance from Pressure Shell.

convection loss is

$$
q _ {c} = h A \theta , \tag {104}
$$

where

$q_{c} =$ heat transferred, Btu/sec, $h =$ free convection heat transfer coefficient, Btu/sec $\cdot ft^2\cdot {}^\circ F,$ $A =$ surface area of tube, ft², $\theta =$ temperature difference between the surface of the tube and the helium, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$

The radiation loss is given by

$$
\begin{array}{l} q _ {R} = \frac {0 . 1 7 3}{3 6 0 0} \times A \tag {105} \\ \times \in \left[ \left(\frac {T _ {\text {t u b e}}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} - \left(\frac {T _ {\text {s i n k}}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} \right], \\ \end{array}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \epsilon = \text {r a d i a t i o n e m i s s i v i t y , d i -} \\ \text {m e n s i o n l e s s ,} \end{array}
$$

$T_{\text{tube}} = \text{absolute temperature of the surface of the tube, } ^o R,$

$T_{\text{sink}} = \text{absolute temperature of the sink (surrounding material)}$ , R.

The total heat loss is

$$
q = q _ {c} + q _ {R}. \tag {106}
$$

Jakob has given Nusselt's number (cf., p. 525, Fig. 25-1, of ref. 14) as a function of the product of Grashof's and Prandtl's numbers. The value of Nusselt's number for the existing helium conditions was taken from Jakob's work, and the free-convection heat transfer coefficient was determined.

The temperature change of the fuel and the metal of the tube per unit time is

$$
\Delta T = \frac {Q - q}{\sum w c _ {p}}, \tag {107}
$$

where

$$
\Delta T = \text {t e m p e r a t u r e} ^ {\prime} \text {c h a n g e}, ^ {\prime} \mathrm {F} / \sec ,
$$

$\sum w c_{p} =$ summation of heat capacities of fuel and metal of tube, Btu/°F,

$$
w = \text {w e i g h t o f m a t e r i a l}, \quad \mathrm {l b},
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} c _ {p} = \text {s p e c i f i c h e a t o f m a t e r i a l}, \\ B t u / 1 b ^ {\cdot} ^ {\circ} F. \end{array}
$$

The data required to make the calculations are listed in the following tabulation; only the portion of the tube that is below the tube sheet is considered:

1. For the metal tube,

$$
\text {A r e a}, A \quad 0. 2 2 6 f t ^ {2}
$$

$$
V o l u m e \quad 1. 8 6 i n. ^ {3}
$$

$$
\text {D e n s i t y}, \rho \quad 0. 3 0 7 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {i n}. ^ {3}
$$

$$
\text {S p e c i f i c} \quad c _ {p} \quad 0. 1 1 \mathrm {B t u} / \mathrm {l b} ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}
$$

2. For the fuel,

$$
\text {V o l u m e} \quad 4. 7 4 \times 1 0 ^ {* 3} f t ^ {3}
$$

$$
\text {D e n s i t y}, \rho \quad 1 8 7 \quad \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}
$$

$$
\text {S p e c i f i c} \quad c _ {p} \quad 0. 2 6 \mathrm {B t u} / \mathrm {l b} ^ {\cdot} \circ F
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \text {T o t a l f u e l v o l u m e} \\ \text {o f e n t i r e s y s t e m} \end{array} 7. 7 5 f t ^ {3}
$$

The temperatures of the fuel at various times after shutdown were calculated by numerical methods for sink temperatures of 1200 and $1300^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . The calculated data for the two sink temperatures are given in Fig. 25.

# FORMATION OF NONPLUGGING SOLIDS IN FUEL TUBE IN THE EVENT OF A SMALL LEAK

In the event of a small leak in a fuel tube, it is possible that the NaK entering the fuel tube will precipitate solids that will adhere to the walls of the fuel tube. This deposit can build up to appreciable thickness before causing any measurable change in the flow through the fuel tube. The temperatures which might exist in such a deposit were calculated by treating the solid deposit as a flat plate, with internal heat generation, cooled on one side by the flowing fuel. The differential equation and boundary conditions governing the heat flow are

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {d ^ {2} \theta}{d x ^ {2}} = - \frac {q ^ {\prime}}{k}, (108) \\ \frac {d \theta}{d x} = 0, \text {a t} x = 0, (109) \\ - k \frac {d \theta}{d x} = h \theta , \quad \text {a t} x = \tau , (110) \\ \end{array}
$$

where

$\theta =$ temperature difference between a point in the solid and the flowing fuel, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{F}$

$x =$ distance from tube wall, ft, $q^{\prime} =$ internal heat generation, Btu/hr·ft³,

$k =$ thermal conductivity of fuel, Btu/hr·ft² (°F/ft),

$\tau =$ thickness of solid deposit, ft, $h =$ heat transfer coefficient from solid to flowing fuel, Btu/hr·ft² $(^{\circ}\mathrm{F} / \mathrm{ft})$

The heat transfer coefficient is given by

$$
h = 0. 0 2 7 \frac {k}{D} \operatorname {R e} ^ {0. 8} \Pr^ {1 / 3} \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) ^ {0. 1 4}, \tag {111}
$$

where

$D =$ equivalent diameter, ft,

Re = Reynolds' number,

$\mathbf{Pr} = \mathbf{Prandtl's}$ number,

$\frac{\mu}{\mu_s} = \text{ratio of viscosity at bulk temperature to viscosity at surface temperature.}$

Two cases will be considered; a tube near the inlet where the fuel temperature is about $1170^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ and the internal heat generation is $1.64 \times 10^{7}$ Btu/hr·ft³ on the reactor center line, and a tube near the outlet where the fuel temperature is about $1490^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ and the internal heat generation is about $0.85 \times 10^{7}$ Btu/hr·ft³ on the reactor center line:

Fuel temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{F}$ 1170 1490

Internal heat generation,

$$
\mathrm {B t u} / \mathrm {h r} ^ {\cdot} \mathrm {f t} ^ {3} \quad 1. 6 4 \times 1 0 ^ {7} \quad 0. 8 5 \times 1 0 ^ {7}
$$

Equivalent diameter, ft 0.0929 0.0929

Reynolds' number 7700 14, 100

Prandtl's number 5.20 2.85

$$
\left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) ^ {0. 1 4}
$$

Heat transfer coef

ficient, Btu/hr\*ft²\*°F 945 1260

The solution of the differential equation, Eq. 108, is

$$
\frac {d \theta}{d x} = - \frac {q ^ {\prime} x}{k} + c _ {1}, \tag {112}
$$

$$
\theta = - \frac {q ^ {\prime} x ^ {2}}{2 k} + c _ {1} x + c _ {2}. \tag {113}
$$

The boundary conditions, Eqs. 109 and 110, give

$$
c _ {1} = 0, \tag {114}
$$

$$
c _ {2} = \frac {q ^ {\prime} \tau^ {2}}{2 k} + \frac {q ^ {\prime} \tau}{h}, \tag {115}
$$

and Eq. 113 becomes

$$
\theta = \frac {q ^ {\prime}}{2 k} \left(\tau^ {2} - x ^ {2}\right) + \frac {q ^ {\prime} \tau}{h}. \tag {116}
$$

The maximum temperature in the solid occurs at $x = 0$ ; therefore

$$
T _ {\text {m a x}} = \theta_ {\text {m a x}} + T _ {f} = \frac {q ^ {\prime} \tau^ {2}}{2 k} + \frac {q ^ {\prime} \tau}{h} + T _ {f}, \tag {117}
$$

where

$T_{\text{max}} = \text{maximum temperature in the solid},$ F,

$T_{f} =$ temperature of the flowing fuel, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$

A tabulation of values of some of the terms of Eq. 117 for values of $\tau$ of 0.1 and 0.2 in. is presented below:

$$
T _ {f}, ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F} \quad 1 1 7 0 \quad 1 4 9 0
$$

$$
\tau , \quad \text {i n .} \quad 0. 1 \quad 0. 2 \quad 0. 1 \quad 0. 2
$$

$$
\frac {q ^ {\prime} \tau^ {2}}{2 k}, ^ {\circ} F \quad 3 8 0 \quad 1 4 1 8 \quad 1 9 7 \quad 7 8 7
$$

$$
\frac {q ^ {\prime} \tau}{h}, ^ {\circ} F \quad 1 4 5 \quad 2 8 9 \quad 5 6 \quad 1 1 2
$$

$$
T _ {\max }, ^ {\circ} F \quad 1 6 9 4 \quad 2 8 7 8 \quad 1 7 4 3 \quad 2 3 8 8
$$

The values for the maximum temperatures of the solid indicate that unless the solids found when NaK and fuel are mixed have a lower melting point than that of Inconel there is danger that a small leak might result in the formation of a solid deposit which would reach a high enough temperature to melt the Inconel tube wall.

# Chapter 3

# FUEL HEAT DISPOSAL SYSTEM

# PERFORMANCE OF FUEL HEAT DISPOSAL SYSTEM

Heat Transfer. The heat added to the fuel in the reactor is disposed of by circulating the fuel to a heat exchanger where it gives up its heat to helium. The hot helium is then passed through a heat exchanger where it gives up its heat to water. The fuel and helium recirculate; the water is dumped. There are actually four fuel-to-helium heat exchangers and four helium-to-water exchangers. These exchangers are arranged (Fig. 1) so that all four fuel-to-helium exchangers are in parallel on the fuel side and all four helium-to-water exchangers are in parallel on the water side. The helium circuits are arranged so that there are two parallel circuits and each circuit contains two fuel-to-helium and two helium-to-water exchangers in series (the exchangers alternate, of course). Figures 26 and 27 show the details of the two types of heat exchangers. The performance of the fuel heat disposal loop over a range of operating conditions is calculated below.

The fuel heat disposal system obeys the following relations:

(1) $Q = W_{f} c_{f} (T_{f}^{\prime} - T_{f})$ ,   
(2) $Q = W_{\text{H e}} c_{\text{H e}} \left(T_{\text{H e}}^{\prime} - T_{\text{H e}}\right)$ ,   
(3) $Q = W_{w} c_{w} (T_{w}^{\prime} - T_{w})$ ,   
(4) $Q = h_{f\text{-He}} A_{f\text{-He}} \theta_{f\text{-He}}$   
(5) $Q = h_{\mathrm{He} + w}A_{\mathrm{He} + w}\theta_{\mathrm{He} + w},$

where

$Q =$ heattransferred,Btu/sec,

$W =$ weight flow, lb/sec,

$T^{\prime} =$ maximum temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F},$

$T =$ minimum temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$

$c =$ specific heat, Btu/1b\*F,

$h =$ heat transfer coefficient, Btu/sec·ft².°F,

$A =$ heat transfer area, ft²,

$\theta =$ effective log mean temperature difference, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$

and the subscripts refer to

$$
\begin{array}{l} f = \text {f u e l}, \\ \mathrm {H e} = \text {h e l i u m}, \\ w = \text {w a t e r}, \\ \end{array}
$$

$f\text{-He} =$ fuel-to-helium heat exchanger, He- $\pmb{\varpi} =$ helium-to-waterheatexchanger.

The heat disposal system is operated with the following restraints and conditions:

1. The fuel flow is held fixed at 28.2 lb/sec.   
2. The water outlet temperature is thermostatically controlled and held fixed at $135^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .   
3. The water inlet temperature will vary with the time of year, but for this analysis it has been assumed to be fixed at $70^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .   
4. The mean temperature of the fuel is fixed because the reactor is self-controlling to a constant mean temperature. The design mean temperature is $1325^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . Calculations were also carried out for mean temperatures of 1225 and $1425^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ to give some indication of the effect of a change in mean fuel temperature.

The helium weight flow is the independent variable; the flow is controlled by variable speed hydraulic motors which drive the two helium blowers. The dependent variables are $Q, W_{w}, T_{f}^{\prime}, T_{f}, T_{He}^{\prime}$ , and $T_{He}$ . Thus there are six unknowns and six equations: the five equations listed above and the one inherent in the fourth condition, namely:

$$
\frac {T _ {f} ^ {\prime} + T _ {f}}{2} = \text {a c o n s t a n t}. \tag {6}
$$

Before these equations can be solved, the heat transfer coefficients and areas must be calculated for the two exchangers. The overall $hA$ 's are

(7) $(hA)_{f\bullet H}$

$$
= \left[ \frac {1}{(h A) _ {f}} + \frac {1}{(h A) _ {\mathrm {H e}}} + \frac {1}{(h A) _ {m}} \right] _ {f - \mathrm {H e}} ^ {- 1}
$$

![](images/49f378c30679a9f82e3258eddc5e0d7c5c1aa29b29f8cc8c0c23bb2bd00dcb3f.jpg)  
SONEL

WELDING NECK FLANGE.</td><td>FORGE STEEL</td><td>ASME SA-181</td><td>GRADE I</td></tr><tr><td>3-in. SCH. 40 PIPE.</td><td>SEAMLESS STEEL</td><td>ASME SA-106</td><td>GRADE A</td></tr><tr><td>TUBES, TUBE BENDS.</td><td>SEAMLESS STEEL</td><td>ASME SA-179</td><td>MIN WALL</td></tr><tr><td>FINS</td><td>COPPER</td><td>COMMERCIAL</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>STAT. HEAD GASKETS.</td><td>DURABLA</td><td>COMMERCIAL</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>SHELL GASKET SMEARED BOTH SIDES WITH PLASTISEAL</td><td>ASBESTOS CLOTH</td><td>COMMERCIAL</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>BOLTING FOR TUBE SHEET AND SHELL FLANGE.</td><td>STAINLESS STEEL</td><td>ASME SA-193 BB</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>NUTS AT TUBE SHEET AND STAT. HEAD COVER PLATE.</td><td>BETH OIL QUENCH</td><td>ASME SA-194</td><td>CLASS 2H</td></tr><tr><td>BOLTING FOR STAT. HEAD COVER PLATE.</td><td>ALLOY STEEL</td><td></td><td></td></tr></table>

and

(8) $(hA)_{\text{He} - w}$

$$
= \left[ \frac {1}{(h A) _ {\mathrm {H e}}} + \frac {1}{(h A) _ {w}} + \frac {1}{(h A) _ {a}} \right] _ {\mathrm {H e} = w} ^ {- 1},
$$

where the subscript $m$ refers to the metal of the tube wall.

The heat transfer coefficients of the fuel are calculated from the curve of ref. 11 (p. 193). The heat transfer coefficient of the water is

(9) $h_{w} = 0.027\frac{k_{w}}{D_{w}}\operatorname{Re}_{w}^{0 \cdot 8}\operatorname{Pr}_{w}^{1 / 3}\left(\frac{\mu}{\mu_{s}}\right)_{w}^{0 \cdot 14}$

(Sieder-Tate relation ${}^{(15)}$ ), where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \begin{array}{l} k = \text {t h e r m a l c o n d u c t i v i t y , B t u / s e c} ^ {\cdot} f t ^ {2} \\ (\circ F / f t), \end{array} \\ D = \text {e q u i v a l e n t} \\ \mathrm {R e} = \text {R e y n o l d s} ^ {\prime} \text {n u m b e r}, \\ \Pr = \text {P r a n d t l} ^ {\prime} \text {s n u m b e r}, \\ \begin{array}{l} \mu = \text {v i s c o s i t y a t b u l k t e m p e r a t u r e}, \\ \quad \mathrm {l b / h r} \cdot \mathrm {f t}, \end{array} \\ \begin{array}{l} \mu_ {s} = \text {v i s c o s i t y a t s u r f a c e t e m p e r a t u r e}, \\ \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \end{array} \\ \end{array}
$$

The liquid-side areas are:

$$
A _ {f} = \pi D _ {f} L _ {f - H e} \tag {10}
$$

and

$$
A _ {w} = \pi D _ {w} L _ {\text {H e - w}}, \tag {11}
$$

where $L$ is the total effective length of the heat exchanger tubes in feet.

The resistance of the metal is

$$
\left[ \frac {1}{(h A) _ {\text {m}}} \right] _ {f - \mathrm {H e}} = \frac {\ln \left(\frac {D _ {o}}{D _ {f}}\right) _ {f - \mathrm {H e}}}{2 \pi \left(k _ {\mathrm {m}}\right) _ {f - \mathrm {H e}} L _ {f - \mathrm {H e}}} \tag {12}
$$

and

$$
\left[ \frac {1}{(h A) _ {m}} \right] _ {\text {H e - w}} = \frac {\ln \left(\frac {D _ {o}}{D _ {f}}\right) _ {\text {H e - w}}}{2 \pi \left(k _ {m}\right) _ {\text {H e - w}} L _ {\text {H e - w}}}, \tag {13}
$$

where $D_{o}$ is tube outside diameter in feet.

The heat transfer coefficients on the gas side are taken from a corre

lation by Kern of data by Jameson and Tate and Cartinhour (cf., p. 555 of ref. 8). Kern's correlation curve may be approximated by the following equation:

(14) $h_{\text{He}}$

$$
= 0. 0 9 2 \frac {k _ {\mathrm {H e}}}{D _ {\mathrm {H e}}} \operatorname {R e} _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {0. 7 2 3} \Pr_ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {1 / 3} \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {0. 1 4},
$$

where $D_{\mathrm{He}}$ for the fin and tube configuration is defined as

$$
D _ {\mathrm {H e}} = \frac {2 \left(A _ {o} + A _ {f}\right)}{\pi (\text {p r o j e c t e d p e r i m e t e r})}, \tag {15}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} A _ {o} = \text {t u b e o u t s i d e b a r e a r e a , f t} ^ {2}, \\ A _ {f} = \text {f i n a r e a , f t} ^ {2}, \end{array}
$$

and the projected perimeter is the sum of all the external distances in the plan view of the finned tubes (ft).

The mass velocity used in the Reynolds' number is computed from the free flow area in a single bank of tubes at right angles to the gas flow. The helium-side heat transfer area is

$$
A _ {\mathrm {H e}} = A _ {o} + \eta_ {f} A _ {f}, \tag {16}
$$

where $\eta_{f}$ is the fin efficiency. (16)

Table 8 lists the pertinent information obtained from the heat exchanger designs (Figs. 26 and 27) and Eqs. 7 through 16. Values are given for three different flow rates of helium and of water; curves were plotted from these values and used in the solution of Eqs. 1 through 6.

The data of Table 8 were used to solve the six simultaneous equations (Eqs. 1 through 6) for various values of the independent variable - helium weight flow. (Inasmuch as the water heat transfer coefficient is a function of the water flow rate, it was necessary to assume a water flow rate for each helium flow rate and solve the equations by iteration.) The results of the solution of Eqs. 1 through 6 are shown in Figs. 28 and 29, where the heat transferred, maximum fuel temperature, and maximum and minimum helium

At design point, 700 kw is removed by the reflector coolant and rod cooling circuits. Therefore, 2300 kw is to be removed by the fuel.

Film Temperature. Because the fuel has a high melting point (about $970^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ ), the minimum fuel film temperature must be calculated to determine the situation with respect to freezing of the fuel film. The minimum fuel film temperature at any point may be calculated from the relation

$$
T _ {f _ {\text {m i n}}} = T _ {f _ {\text {b u l k}}} - \frac {Q}{(h A) _ {f}}, \tag {17}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} T _ {f _ {\min }} = \text {m i n i m u m f u e l f i l m t e m p e r -} \\ \text {a t u r e a t t h e p o i n t i n} \\ \text {q u e s t i o n ,} ^ {\circ} F, \end{array}
$$

![](images/90dac0698a8c8479a066bf178bcd9446d10a54e56f5e8abae4f953078cff3861.jpg)  
Fig. 29. Maximum and Minimum Helium Temperature vs. Helium Volumetric Flow.

$T_{f_{\text{bulk}}} = \text{bulk fuel temperature at the point in question, }^{\circ} \text{F.}$

The other symbols mean the same as they did previously, except that the fuel heat transfer coefficient, $h_f$ , must be evaluated locally, that is, at the point in question. Several critical points were investigated in the fuel-to-helium exchanger, and a plot of the lowest of the temperatures vs. helium volumetric flow (and various mean fuel temperatures) is shown in Fig. 30a.

Pressure Drop. The pressure drops in the fuel and in the helium have been calculated for the exchangers. The fuel pressure drop was calculated by using the customary Fanning equation (11) for the straight sections, and the curves presented by Cox and Germano (12) for the bends, exists, and entrances. Since the fuel flow is constant and the properties do not vary substantially in the range of mean fuel temperatures considered, the

![](images/dae030b02b4e56037724de317ded2d74c7a78703011993f05189a80e5daa9e6a.jpg)  
Fig. 30. Minimum Fuel Film Temperature and Helium Pressure Drop vs. Helium Volumetric Flow.

fuel pressure drop is constant and equal to about 17 psi.

The pressure drop in the helium was calculated by the method given by Gunter and Shaw: (17)

$\mu_{s} =$ viscosity at surface temperatures, lb/hr·ft,

$$
D _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {\prime} = \begin{array}{l} \text {e q u i v a l e n t d i a m e t e r (c f . ,} \\ \text {E q . 1 9) ， f t .} \end{array}
$$

The equivalent diameter in the Gunter-Shaw correlation is

$$
D _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {\prime} = \frac {4 \times \text {n e t f r e e v o l u m e}}{A _ {f} + A _ {o}}, \tag {19}
$$

where $A_{f}$ and $A_{o}$ have the same meanings as previously.

The values of $D_{\mathrm{He}}^{\prime}$ are 0.0438 ft for the fuel-to-helium exchangers and 0.1365 ft for the helium-to-water exchangers. The friction factor, $f$ , which is a function of Reynolds' number, is evaluated from the Gunter-Shaw correlation. The helium pressure drop is plotted against helium volumetric flow for both exchangers in Fig. 30b. The helium pressure drop is not greatly affected by the mean fuel temperature, and thus the curves of Fig. 30b are valid for the range of mean fuel temperature considered.

The pressure drops in the fuel and in the helium for the remainder of the fuel and helium circuits have been calculated by using the Fanning equation and the Cox and Germano data. Figure 1 shows the pressure at various points in the fuel circuit. The fuel pressure drop external to the heat exchangers is 40 psi; the total fuel pressure drop is 57 psi (Fig. 2 shows the fuel piping). Figure 31 shows the

$$
\Delta P = 5. 3 0 5 \times 1 0 ^ {- 1 0} \frac {f G _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {2} L _ {p}}{D _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {\prime} s \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) _ {\mathrm {H e}}} \left(\frac {D _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {\prime}}{S _ {t}}\right) ^ {0. 4} \left(\frac {S _ {l}}{S _ {t}}\right) ^ {0. 6}, \tag {18}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta P = \text {p r e s s u r e} \quad \text {d r o p}, \quad \text {i n .} \quad \text {o f} \quad H _ {2} 0, \\ f = \text {f r i c t i o n} \\ \begin{array}{l} G _ {\text {H e}} = \text {h e l i u m u n i t w e i g h t f l o w ,} \\ \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \text {l b / s e c} ^ {2}, \end{array} \\ L _ {p} = \text {l e n g t h o f h e l i u m f l o w p a t h , f t}, \\ s = \text {a v e r a g e h e l i u m s p e c i f i c g r a v i t y}, \\ S _ {t} = \text {t r a n s v e r s e p i t c h}, f t, \\ S _ {l} = \text {l o n g i t u d i n a l p i t c h}, f t, \\ \begin{array}{l} \mu = \text {v i s c o s i t y a t b u l k t e m p e r a t u r e}, \\ \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \text {l b / h r} ^ {\cdot} f t, \end{array} \\ \end{array}
$$

helium ducting for the fuel circuit. The pressure drop external to the heat exchangers and the total pressure drop are plotted against helium volumetric flow in Fig. 32.

# TEMPERATURE IN FUEL SYSTEM BECAUSE OF AFTERHEAT IN THE EVENT OF COMPLETE PUMP FAILURE

If there were a complete power failure and all the fuel remained in

![](images/4da2e0e9ce2e0717cc981d04d389e739470b2a8994aadfdefe44c87d0ac5d6bf.jpg)

![](images/b39704436fe1146725f8e22fd25507c90dd69a3e9c6fe512d707ff42c63f5fea.jpg)  
Fig. 31. Helium Loop for Fuel Circuit.

![](images/ba611ff96f55759e9d451e428fca7c4a7c2800cb8e1a0b749d25fdf65b148428.jpg)  
Fig. 32. Helium Pressure Drop vs. Helium Volumetric Flow.

the system, it would be desirable to know the maximum temperature the fuel would attain (Fig. 1). The temperature rise in the fuel will be due to internal heat generation. However, since the heat generation rate decreases with time after shutdown, at some temperature an equilibrium will be reached between the heat generated and the heat lost from the system. The maximum fuel temperature will be attained at equilibrium. As soon as the maximum has been reached, the temperature will decrease.

The total heat generation rate of the fuel is given as a function of

time after shutdown in chap. 9. Heat is lost from the components of the fuel system by free convection to the helium in the pits and by thermal radiation to the equipment and walls. This heat loss is given in chap. 6.

The change in temperature of the fuel and metal of each fuel system component per unit time is

$$
\Delta T = \frac {Q _ {f} - Q _ {l}}{\sum w c _ {p}} \tag {20}
$$

and

(21) $Q$

$$
= \frac {\text {v o l u m e o f f u e l i n c o m p o n e n t}}{\text {t o t a l f u e l v o l u m e}} \times Q _ {f} ,
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta T = \text {t e m p e r a t u r e} ^ {\prime} \text {c h a n g e}, ^ {\prime} F / \sec , \\ \begin{array}{l} Q _ {f} = \text {t o t a l h e a t g e n e r a t i o n r a t e i n} \\ \text {t h e f u e l , B t u / s e c ,} \end{array} \\ \begin{array}{l} Q = \text {h e a t g e n e r a t i o n r a t e i n t h e f u l e} \\ \text {i n t h e c o m p o n e n t , B t u / s e c ,} \end{array} \\ \begin{array}{l} Q _ {l} = \text {h e a t l o s s f r o m t h e c o m p o n e n t ,} \\ \text {B t u / s e c ,} \end{array} \\ \begin{array}{l} \sum w c _ {p} = h e a t c a p a c i t y o f c o m p o n e n t \\ \text {p l u s f u e l , B t u / ^ {\circ} F ,} \end{array} \\ w = \text {w e i g h t o f m a t e r i a l , l b ,} \\ \begin{array}{l} c _ {p} = \text {s p e c i f i c h e a t o f m a t e r i a l}, \\ \hskip 1 4. 2 2 6 3 7 8 p t \mathrm {B t u / l b} ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}. \end{array} \\ \end{array}
$$

The most critical points in the fuel system under the condition postulated are those with the smallest surface-to-volume ratio. The components in this category are the reactor (Fig. 9), the surge tanks (Fig. 33), and the 2-in. pipe lines.

Table 9 gives the data needed to calculate the temperature rise in these components.

![](images/6414b5fd81328188be1db15a54963db5b8554cccb1eb254c77df4632bb14c915.jpg)  
Fig. 33. Fuel Surge Tank.

For the metal

$$
c _ {p} = 0. 1 1 \mathrm {B t u} / \mathrm {l b} ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F},
$$

$$
\rho = 0. 3 0 7 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {i n}. ^ {3},
$$

and for the fuel

$$
c _ {p} = 0. 2 6 \mathrm {B t u / l b} ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F},
$$

$$
\rho = 1 8 7 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}.
$$

The total volume of fuel in the system was taken to be $7.75\mathrm{ft}^3$ and the sink temperature was taken as $130^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ or $590^{\circ}\mathrm{R}$ .

Curves of temperature vs. time after shutdown are shown in Fig. 34.

# Chapter 4

# REFLECTOR COOLANT HEAT DISPOSAL SYSTEM

The heat added to the reflector coolant (NaK) in the reactor is disposed of by circulating the NaK to a heat exchanger where it gives up its heat to helium. The hot helium is then passed through a heat exchanger where it gives up its heat to water. The NaK and helium recirculate; the water is dumped. There are two NaK-to-helium exchangers and two helium-to-water exchangers arranged (Fig. 1) so that the liquid sides of each pair of exchangers are in parallel. The helium circuit consists of two parallel circuits, and each circuit contains one NaK-to-helium and one helium-to-water heat exchanger in series. Figures 35 and 36 show the details of the two types of heat exchangers. The performance of the NaK heat disposal loop over a range of operating conditions is calculated below.

The NaK heat disposal syster obeys the following relations:

(1) $Q = W_{\text{NaK}} c_{\text{NaK}} \left(T_{\text{NaK}}' - T_{\text{NaK}}\right)$ ,   
(2) $Q = W_{\text{He}} c_{\text{He}} \left(T_{\text{He}}^{\prime} - T_{\text{He}}\right)$ ,   
(3) $Q = W_{w} c_{w} (T_{w}^{\prime} - T_{w})$ ,   
(4) $Q = h_{\mathrm{NaK - He}} A_{\mathrm{NaK - He}} \theta_{\mathrm{NaK - He}}$   
(5) $Q = h_{\mathrm{He - w}} A_{\mathrm{He - w}} \theta_{\mathrm{He - w}}$

where

$Q =$ heat transforred, Btu/sec, $W =$ weight flow, lb/sec,

$T^{\prime} = \text{maximum temperature}, {}^{\circ}\mathrm{F},$

$T =$ minimum temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$

$h =$ heat transfor coefficient, Btu/sec·ft² (°F/ft),

$A =$ heat transfer area, ft²,

$\mathcal{E} =$ effective log mean temperature difference, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$

and the subscripts refer to

$$
\mathrm {N a K} = \mathrm {N a K},
$$

$$
\mathrm {H e} = \text {h e l i u m},
$$

$$
w = \text {w a t e r},
$$

NaK-He = NaK-to-helium heat exchanger,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \text {H e - w = h e l i u m - t o - w a t e r h e a t e x -} \\ \text {c h a n g e r .} \end{array}
$$

The heat disposal system is operated with the following restraints and conditions:

1. The NaK flow is held fixed at 23 lb/sec.   
2. The helium volumetric flow per exchanger is held fixed at 2000 cfm at blower inlet temperature. This fixes $W_{\text{He}}$ once $T_{\text{He}}$ is known.   
3. The water outlet temperature is thermostatically controlled and is held fixed at $100^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .   
4. The water inlet temperature will vary with the time of the year, but for this analysis it has been assumed to be fixed at $70^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

The heat transferred is chosen as the independent variable in the system, since it is determined by the heat added to the NaK in the reactor. This leaves as dependent variables $T_{\text{NaK}}'$ , $T_{\text{NaK}}$ , $T_{\text{He}}'$ , $T_{\text{He}}$ , and $W_{\text{p}}$ . There are therefore five unknowns and five equations (Eqs. 1 through 5). Before these equations can be solved, the heat transfer coefficients and areas must be calculated for the two heat exchangers.

The over-all $hA$ 's are:

(6) $(hA)_{\mathrm{NaK - Hc}}$

$$
= \left[ \frac {1}{(h A)} _ {\mathrm {N a K}} + \frac {1}{(h A)} _ {\mathrm {H e}} + \frac {1}{(h A)} _ {m} \right] _ {\mathrm {N a K - H e}} ^ {- 1}
$$

and

(7) $(hA)_{\text{H e - } w}$

$$
= \left[ \frac {1}{(h A)} _ {\mathrm {H e}} + \frac {1}{(h A)} _ {w} + \frac {1}{(h A)} _ {m} \right] _ {\mathrm {H e} - w} ^ {- 1},
$$

where $m$ refers to the metal of the tube wall.

The liquid-side heat transfer coefficients are:

(8) $h_{\mathrm{NaK}} = \frac{k_{\mathrm{NaK}}}{D_{\mathrm{NaK}}} (7.0 + 0.025 \mathrm{Pe}^{0.8})$

![](images/77ede2d190c9f793d291fff767197a20924ed9e589890528d6ff1e92bf6d8529.jpg)

MATERIAL AND MATERIAL SPECIFICATIONS

COVER B, TUBE SHEET STAT HD R&#x27;S</td><td>FIRE BOX STEEL</td><td>ASTM-A-285</td><td>GRADE C</td></tr><tr><td>1/2-in. ISO-1D WELDING NECK FLANGE</td><td>FORCE STEEL</td><td>ASTM-A-181</td><td>GRADE 1</td></tr><tr><td>1/2-in. SCH. 40 PIPE</td><td>SEAMLESS STEEL</td><td>ASTM-A-105</td><td>GRADE A</td></tr><tr><td>TUBES</td><td>SEAMLESS STEEL</td><td>ASTM-A-179</td><td>MIN WALL</td></tr><tr><td>FINS</td><td>COPPER</td><td>COMMERCIAL</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>STAT. HD. GASKETS</td><td>DURABLE</td><td>COMMERCIAL</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>SHELL GASKET AT TUBE SHEET</td><td>1/8 in TH/K ASBESTOS CLOTH</td><td>COMMERCIAL</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>TAP BOLTS AT BLANKING-OFF BAFFLE</td><td>ALLOY STEEL</td><td>COMMERCIAL</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>BOLTING AT STAT. HD. AND TUBE SHEET</td><td>ALLOY STEEL</td><td>ASTM-A-93 B-7</td><td>125000 TS 105000 YP</td></tr><tr><td>TIE RODS, SPACERS AND NUTS</td><td>STEEL</td><td>COMMERCIAL</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>NUTS AT TUBE SHEET AND COVER PLATE</td><td>BETH OIL QUENCH</td><td>ASTM-A-194</td><td>CLASS 2H</td></tr></table>

(Lyon equation $(6))$ and

$$
h _ {w} = 0. 0 2 7 \frac {k _ {w}}{D _ {w}} \operatorname {R e} _ {w} ^ {0. 8} \Pr_ {w} ^ {1 / 3} \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) _ {w} ^ {0. 1 4} \tag {9}
$$

(Sieder-Tate equation(15)), where

$$
\begin{array}{l} k = \text {t h e r m a l c o n d u c t i v i t y , B t u / s e c} \cdot f t ^ {2} \\ (\circ F / f t), \end{array}
$$

$$
D = \text {e q u i v a l e n t} \quad \text {d i a m e t e r}, \quad f t,
$$

$$
\mathrm {P e} = \text {P e c l e t} ^ {\prime} \text {s n u m b e r ,}
$$

$$
\mathrm {R e} = \text {R e y n o l d s} ^ {\prime} \text {n u m b e r},
$$

$$
P r = \text {P r a n d t l} ^ {\prime} \text {s n u m b e r},
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mu = \text {v i s c o s i t y a t b u l k t e m p e r a t u r e}, \\ \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \text {l b / h r} ^ {\cdot} f t, \end{array}
$$

$$
\mu_ {s} = \text {v i s c o s i t y a t s u r f a c e t e m p e r a - t u r e , l b / h r} \cdot \text {f t}.
$$

The liquid-side areas are

$$
A _ {\mathrm {N a K}} = \pi D _ {\mathrm {N a K}} L _ {\mathrm {N a K - H e}} \tag {10}
$$

and

$$
A _ {w} = \pi D _ {w} L _ {\text {H e - w}} \tag {11}
$$

where $L$ is the total effective length of the heat exchanger tubes in feet.

The resistance of the metal is

$$
\left[ \frac {1}{(h A) _ {m}} \right] _ {\mathrm {N a K - H e}} = \frac {\ln \left(\frac {D _ {o}}{D _ {\mathrm {N a K}}}\right) _ {\mathrm {N a K - H e}}}{2 \pi \left(k _ {m}\right) _ {\mathrm {N a K - H e}} L _ {\mathrm {N a K - H e}}} \tag {12}
$$

and

$$
\left[ \frac {1}{(h A) _ {m}} \right] _ {\text {H e - w}} = \frac {\ln \left(\frac {D _ {o}}{D _ {w}}\right) _ {\text {H e - w}}}{2 \pi (k _ {m}) _ {\text {H e - w}} L _ {\text {H e - w}}} \tag {13}
$$

where $D_{\alpha}$ is the tube diameter in feet.

The heat transfer coefficients on the gas side are calculated in a manner identical to that used for the fuel heat disposal system calculations (cf., Eqs. 14, 15, and 16 of chap. 3).

Table 10 contains a list of pertinent information obtained from heat exchanger designs (Figs. 35 and 36) and the heat transfer relations discussed above. Values are given for four different flow rates of helium and four different flow rates of water; curves were plotted from these values and used in the solution of Eqs. 1 through 5.

The data of Table 10 were used to solve the five simultaneous equations (Eqs. 1 through 5) for various values of the independent variable - heat

transferred. Inasmuch as the helium volumetric flow is constant (see restraint 2), the helium weight flow cannot be precisely determined until the helium temperature at the blower $(T_{\mathrm{He}})$ is known. It was necessary therefore to assume a helium weight flow for each power and to solve the equations by iteration. The results of the solution of Eqs. 1 through 5 are shown in Figs. 37 and 38, in which the minimum and maximum NaK and helium temperatures are plotted against heat transferred. The mean NaK temperature is also shown in Fig. 37.

The pressure drops in the NaK and in the helium have been calculated for the exchangers. The NaK pressure drop was calculated with the use of the customary Fanning equation (11) for the

# Chapter 5

# ROD AND INSTRUMENT COOLING SYSTEM

# COOLING OF THE CONTROL RODS AND INSTRUMENTS

There are six vertical holes in the reactor of the ARE (Fig. 16) into which a regulating rod, three safety rods, and two fission chambers can be lowered, when required. Helium is blown through the annular passages between the rods and instruments and the reactor to cool the various surfaces present. In the case of the rods, this cooling is desirable because the guide vanes on the rods slide in contact with the sleeve in the reactor; considerable uncertainty exists as to what would happen if this sliding contact were to be made at the high temperatures in the reactor. The instruments require cooling because the electrical insulators used in their construction emit false signals at temperatures above about $700^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . The rod and instrument cooling circuit consists of two blowers, each of 1000 cfm capacity, which blow helium through the annular passages in the reactor holes, and a heat exchanger which removes from the helium the heat picked up in the reactor holes. Although the blowers have a total capacity of 2000 cfm, they are operated so that the helium flow is 1000 cfm. Figure 1 shows a diagram of the rod and instrument cooling system. Figure 42 shows the dimensions of the helium passages through the reactor. Figure 17 shows a cross section of the helium passages and details of the sleeve in the reactor. This sleeve, as may be seen, is actually triple-walled. The helium passes between the inner wall and the rod or instrument in question. The space between the inner wall and the middle wall is packed with diatomaceous earth, which serves as thermal insulation between the reactor and the rod or instrument hole. NaK from the reflector coolant circuit flows between the middle and outer wall and provides some cooling because it is

colder than the surrounding sections of the reactor core. Since the 1000 cfm of helium available may be apportioned differently to the various holes, the cooling of the various holes was investigated for a range of helium flows. Also, at the time of the calculations, the mean NaK temperature was unknown and, consequently, a range of mean NaK temperatures was investigated. The cooling of the various rods and instruments will be considered separately and in the following order: safety rods, regulating rod, fission chambers. The division of the total flow among the different holes will be discussed last.

![](images/7a00dcfa01f0bfe1bcb4bd936845a8679979d6c15efd07204b650025b5695da5.jpg)

![](images/da825db53eb5da96e52233a12e277a45aafe94cf9cafb529eb87f926c988bb80.jpg)  
Fig. 42. Rod and Instrument Sleeves.

# COOLING OF THE SAFETY RODS

During normal reactor operation, the safety rods are not in the reactor but are withdrawn into sleeves above the reactor. The safety rods themselves are in a cool area, and the only cooling problem, then, is that of cooling the sleeve in the reactor. The equations governing this process are quite straightforward and are listed below:

(1) $Q = W_{C_p} (T_{H_e}' - T_{H_e})$   
(2) $Q = hA\theta + Q_r,$   
(3) $hA = \left[\frac{1}{(hA)_{\textbf{NaK}}} + \frac{1}{(hA)_{m}}\right]$

$$
\left. + \frac {1}{(h A) _ {I}} + \frac {1}{(h A) _ {n}} + \frac {1}{(h A) _ {\mathrm {H e}}} \right] ^ {- 1},
$$

where

$Q =$ heat transferred to helium, Btu/sec,

$W =$ weight flow of helium, lb/sec,

$c_{p} =$ specific heat of helium, Btu/lb $^{\circ}$ F,

$T^{\prime} =$ maximum temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$

$T =$ minimum temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$

$h =$ heat transfer coefficient, Btu/sec $\cdot ft^2$ (°F/ft),

$A =$ heat transfer area, ft²,

$\theta =$ effective log mean temperature difference between the NaK and the helium,

$Q_{r} =$ heat generated in the sleeve by nuclear radiation, Btu/sec,

and the subscripts

He = heli um,

$\mathrm{NaK} = \mathrm{NaK}$

$m =$ middle metal wallin sleeve,

$I =$ insulation (diatomaceous earth), $n =$ inner metal wall.

The various terms in Eq. 3 may be evaluated as follows:

(4) $h_{\mathrm{NaK}} = \frac{k_{\mathrm{NaK}}}{D_{\mathrm{NaK}}}\left(5.8 + 0.020\mathrm{Pe}^{0.8}\right)$ ,

where

$k =$ thermal conductivity, Btu/sec·ft² (°F/ft),

$D =$ equivalent diameter (four times hydraulic radius), ft,

$\mathbf{Pe} = \mathbf{P}$ eclet's number;

(5) $A_{\textbf{NaK}} = d_m^{\prime}h$

where

$d^{\prime} =$ tube outside diameter, ft, $L =$ length of tube height of pressure shell, ft;

$$
\frac {1}{(h A) _ {m}} = \frac {\ln \frac {d ^ {\prime} m}{d _ {m}}}{2 \pi k _ {m} L}, \tag {6}
$$

where

$d =$ tube inside diameter, ft;

$$
\frac {1}{(h A) _ {I}} = \frac {\ln \frac {d _ {m}}{d _ {m} ^ {\prime}}}{2 \pi k _ {I} L}; \tag {7}
$$

$$
\frac {1}{(h A) _ {n}} = \frac {\ln \frac {d _ {n} ^ {\prime}}{d _ {n}}}{2 \pi k _ {n} L}; \tag {8}
$$

(9) $h_{\mathrm{He}} = 0.023 \frac{k_{\mathrm{He}}}{D} \mathrm{Re}^{0.8} \mathrm{Pr}^{0.4}$ ,

$$
f o r \mathrm {R e} \geq 2 1 0 0,
$$

$$
h _ {\mathrm {H e}} = 1. 8 6 \frac {k _ {\mathrm {H e}}}{D _ {\mathrm {H e}}} \operatorname {R e} ^ {1 / 3} \Pr^ {1 / 3} \left(\frac {D _ {\mathrm {H e}}}{L}\right) ^ {1 / 3},
$$

$$
f o r \mathrm {R e} <   2 1 0 0;
$$

(10) $A_{\mathbf{H}\mathbf{e}} = \pi d_{n}L.$

Equations 1 through 10 and the dimensions shown in Figs. 42 and 9 were used to calculate the exit temperature of the helium and the heat transferred to the helium; an inlet helium temperature of $150^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ was assumed. The properties of helium were evaluated at the mean helium temperature. The range of helium weight flows considered was from 0.005 to 0.100 lb/sec, and the calculations were made for mean NaK temperatures of 1000, 1150, and $1300^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . The results of the computations are shown in Figs. 43, 44, and 45.

The maximum temperature of the sleeve wall can be calculated from the

![](images/606781a55a5a9c30d0e5377244603faadbc915f2ebdb28224378eff5a09899fb.jpg)  
Fig. 43. Safety Rod Cooling at a NaK Temperature of $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

following equation:

$$
\left(T _ {n}\right) _ {\max } = T _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {\prime} + \frac {Q}{\left(h A\right) _ {\mathrm {H e}}} \tag {11}
$$

The helium heat transfer coefficient, $h$ , in Eq. 11 is evaluated by means of Eq. 9, with the helium properties corresponding to the maximum helium temperature being used. The maximum wall temperature was evaluated by using Eq. 11, and the results are shown in Figs. 43, 44, and 45.

# COOLING OF THE REGULATING ROD

During normal operation, the position of the regulating rod may vary from a position completely out of the reactor to a position where the midpoint of the stainless steel slug is coincident with the center line

![](images/258b9e2ba817f92b795b4bd093034c8a2c9d4be84f8b2e0d857f15008930dba3.jpg)  
Fig. 44. Safety Rod Cooling at a NaK Temperature of $1150^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

![](images/bb5e695c075872b3d4a09494930d399809edeeebd1c6faf3352e74d1830763dd.jpg)  
Fig. 45. Safety Rod Cooling at a NaK Temperature of $1300^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

of the reactor. When the regulating rod is completely out of the reactor, the situation is exactly the same as that analyzed in the previous section on the safety rods, the triple-walled sleeve being identical for the two types of rods. When the regulating rod is in the reactor, the situation is more complex; the case of the regulating rod all the way into the reactor, which is the extreme condition,

will be analyzed. The regulating rod hole will be divided into three sections in the reactor (Fig. 46). The top section of the rod hole is the section that contains the portion of the regulating rod which is in the reactor but contains no appreciable amount of neutron and gamma absorber. The middle section of the rod hole is the section that contains the portion of the regulating rod which contains

![](images/00a36386829275a578623899c3ad2cae7c4682589a8838a1326ca9dbe7f7a985.jpg)

![](images/52a9d2392b53fc3b79f3a99bc1aedb6ce0f97328c9e8bd99e4fdedd20955f538.jpg)  
Fig. 46. Regulating Rod.

the heavy stainless steel slug. The bottom section of the rod hole is the section that contains no regulating rod. The equations governing the cooling process in the three sections of the rod hole are

$$
\begin{array}{l} Q _ {i} = W _ {C _ {p}} \left(T _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {\prime} - T _ {\mathrm {H e}}\right) _ {i}, \tag {12} \\ i = 1, 2, 3, \\ \end{array}
$$

where the symbols have the same meanings as given previously and the subscript $i$ refers to the sections of the regulating rod hole:

$$
\begin{array}{l} 1 = \text {t o p} \\ 2 = \text {m i d d l e} \\ 3 = \text {b o t t o m} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
Q _ {i} = \left(h A \theta + Q _ {r} + q\right) _ {i}, \tag {13}
$$

where

$q =$ the heat which must be removed from the rod, Btu/sec;

$$
q _ {i} = \left(q _ {r} + q _ {R}\right) _ {i}, \tag {14}
$$

where

$q_{r} =$ heat generated in the rod by nuclear radiation, Btu/sec,

$q_{R} =$ heat transferred from the sleeve to the rod by thermal radiation, Btu/sec;

it may be noted that $(q_{r})_{i = 1} = 0$ and $q_{i = 3} = 0;$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \left(h A\right) _ {i} = \left[ \frac {1}{\left(h A\right) _ {\mathrm {N a K}}} + \frac {1}{\left(h A\right) _ {\mathrm {m}}} \right. \tag {15} \\ \left. + \frac {1}{(h A) _ {I}} + \frac {1}{(h A) _ {n}} + \frac {1}{(h A) _ {\mathrm {H} e}} \right] _ {i} ^ {- 1}, \\ \end{array}
$$

where the various terms are evaluated exactly as the corresponding terms in Eq. 3;

$$
\begin{array}{l} \left(q _ {R}\right) _ {i} = \frac {0 . 1 7 3}{3 6 0 0} \frac {a _ {i}}{\frac {1}{\epsilon} + \left(\frac {a _ {i}}{A _ {n}}\right) \left(\frac {1}{\epsilon} - 1\right)} \tag {16} \\ \left(q _ {R}\right) _ {i = 3} = 0, \\ \end{array}
$$

where

$a =$ surface area of control rod, ft², $t^{\prime} =$ maximum rod temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ abs, $t =$ minimum rod temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ abs, $\epsilon =$ emissivity.

It may be noted that the arithmetical-average temperature is used in Eq. 16 to evaluate the heat transferred by thermal radiation. This is only an approximation, but a check has indicated that it is not too greatly in error. Equations 12 through 16 and the dimensions shown in Figs. 42, 46, and 47 were used to calculate for the various sections the exit temperature of the helium and the heat transferred to the helium; an inlet temperature to the rod hole of $150^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ was assumed. The properties of helium were evaluated at the mean temperatures, and the values of $Q_{r}$ and $q_{r}$ are shown in Fig. 48. The range of helium weight flows considered was 0.015 to 0.025 lb/sec; average NaK temperatures of 1000, 1150, and 1300 were used, as before. The results of the calculations are shown in Figs. 49, 50, 51, and 52, which show the helium temperature and the heat transferred to the helium at the end of sections 2 and 3 plotted against helium flow for the various NaK temperatures.

The maximum temperature of the sleeve and the rod can be calculated from the following equations:

$$
\left(T _ {n}\right) _ {\max } = \left(T _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {\prime}\right) _ {i = 3} + \left[ \frac {Q}{(h A) _ {\mathrm {H e}}} \right] _ {i = 3} \tag {17}
$$

and

$$
\left(t\right) _ {\max } = \left(T _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {\prime}\right) _ {i = 2} + \left[ \frac {Q}{(h A) _ {\mathrm {H e}}} \right] _ {i = 2}. \tag {18}
$$

Again, the helium heat transfer coefficients are evaluated locally.

$$
\left[ \left(\frac {T _ {n} + T _ {n} ^ {\prime}}{2 0 0}\right) ^ {4} - \left(\frac {t + t ^ {\prime}}{2 0 0}\right) ^ {4} \right] _ {i = 1, 2},
$$

The results of the evaluations of Eqs. 17 and 18 are also shown in Figs. 49, 50, and 51.

Each instrument hole is divided into sections in much the same fashion as the regulating rod hole was divided. The top section of the hole is the portion which contains the fission chamber; the

where the subscript $i$ refers to

l = top section

and

2 = bottom section;

$$
Q _ {i} = \left(h A \theta + q _ {R}\right) _ {i}, \tag {20}
$$

where the symbols have the same meanings as before;

$$

where the various terms are also evaluated the same as in Eq. 3, except that the heat transfer coefficient in an annulus is given by

# HELIUM PRESSURE DROPS

The helium pressure drops in the safety rod, regulating rod, and instrument sleeves are calculated by

$$
h = 1. 0 2 \frac {k _ {\mathrm {H e}}}{D _ {\mathrm {H e}}} \operatorname {R e} ^ {0 \cdot 4 5} \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) ^ {0 \cdot 1 4} \left(\frac {D _ {\mathrm {H e}}}{l}\right) ^ {0 \cdot 4} \left(\frac {d _ {n}}{d _ {f}}\right) ^ {0 \cdot 8} \operatorname {G r} ^ {0 \cdot 0 5}, \quad \text {f o r R e <   2 1 0 0 ,} \tag {22}
$$

where

$$
\mu = \begin{array}{l} \text {d y n a m i c v i s c o s i t y e v a l u a t e d a t} \\ \text {t h e m e a n t e m p e r a t u r e o f h e l i u m ,} \\ \text {l b / s e c ^ {\cdot} f t ,} \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mu_ {s} = \text {d y n a m i c v i s c o s i t y e v a l u a t e d a t} \\ \text {i n n e r w a l l t e m p e r a t u r e ,} \\ \text {l b / s e c} \cdot f t, \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} l = \text {l e n g t h o f s e c t i o n , f t}, \\ \begin{array}{l} d _ {f} = \text {o u t s i d e d i a m e t e r o f f i s s i o n} \\ \text {c h a m b e r , f t ,} \end{array} \\ \mathrm {G r} = \text {G r a s h o f n u m b e r}; \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
q _ {R} = \frac {0 . 1 7 3}{3 6 0 0} \frac {a _ {f}}{\frac {1}{\epsilon_ {f}} + \left(\frac {a _ {f}}{A _ {n}}\right) \left(\frac {1}{\epsilon} - 1\right)} \tag {23}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \begin{array}{l} a _ {f} = \text {s u r f a c e a r e a o f f i s s i o n c h a m b e r ,} \\ \quad \mathrm {f t} ^ {2}, \end{array} \\ t _ {f} = \text {m i n i m u m f i s s i o n c h a m b e r t e m p e r a - t u r e ,} ^ {\circ} F \text {a b s ,} \\ t _ {f} ^ {\prime} = \text {m a x i m u m f i s s i o n c h a m b e r t e m p e r a - t u r e ,} ^ {\circ} F \text {a b s ,} \\ \begin{array}{l} \varepsilon_ {f} = \text {e m i s s i v i t y o f f i s s i o n c h a m b e r}, \\ \varepsilon = \text {e m i s s i v i t y o f t u b e}. \end{array} \\ \end{array}
$$

The cooling requirement calculations for the fission chambers are made with the assumption that the bottom of each instrument (excluding nose) is at the center line of the core. Also, the maximum temperature of the instrument is taken to be $600^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , and the inlet temperature of helium is taken to be $150^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . The outside diameter of the instrument is 1.25 in., and the

using various helium flows and a NaK temperature of $1170^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . The equations governing the pressure drops are

$$
\Delta P _ {f} = 4 f \frac {l}{D} \frac {\rho v ^ {2}}{2 g}, \tag {24}
$$

$$
\Delta P _ {c} = X \frac {\rho v ^ {2}}{2 g}, \tag {25}
$$

$$
\left[ \left(\frac {T _ {n} + T _ {n} ^ {\prime}}{2 0 0}\right) ^ {4} - \left(\frac {t _ {f} + t _ {f} ^ {\prime}}{2 0 0}\right) ^ {4} \right]
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta P _ {e} = \frac {\rho (v _ {1} - v _ {2}) ^ {2}}{2 g}, (26) \\ \Delta P _ {y} = 4 f \frac {l _ {e}}{D} \frac {\rho v ^ {2}}{2 g}. (27) \\ \end{array}
$$

The symbols have the following meanings and units:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta P = \text {p r e s s u r e} \quad \text {d r o p}, \quad \mathrm {l b / f t} ^ {2}, \\ f = \text {f r i c t i o n} \\ l = \text {l e n g t h o f s e c t i o n , f t}, \\ l _ {e} = \text {e q u i v a l e n t} \\ \check {D} = \text {h y d r a u l i c} \\ v = \text {a v e r a g e} \\ \begin{array}{l} g = \text {g r a v i t a t i o n a l a c c e l e r a t i o n}, \\ 3 2. 2 f t / \sec^ {2}, \end{array} \\ X = \text {c o n s t a n t}, \text {d i m e n s i o n l e s s}, \\ \end{array}
$$

$\nu_{1} =$ average linear velocity upstream, ft/sec,   
$\nu_{2} =$ average linear velocity downstream, ft/sec.

The meanings of the subscripts are the following:

$$
c = \text {c o n t r a c t i o n},
$$

$f =$ friction,   
$e =$ expansion,   
$\mathbf{y} =$ elbow.

The friction factor, $f$ , is given by

(28) $f = \frac{16}{\mathrm{Re}}$ for Re 2100

$$
f = 0. 0 0 1 4 + \frac {0 . 1 2 5}{\left(\mathrm {R e}\right) ^ {0 . 3 2}} ,
$$

for $\mathrm{Re} > 2100$

However, for an annulus

(29) $f = \frac{6}{\mathrm{Re}}$ for $\mathrm{Re} \leq 2100$ .

Values for $X$ are given by McAdams (cf., p. 122 of ref. 11), and $l_{e}$ values are taken from ref. 13.

![](images/ce8e3069a55be61138b0ea5c565af059b9fb1ec400a6f7ad46b3dbe48ce7b2eb.jpg)  
Fig. 54. Instrument Cooling.

Each sleeve is equipped with a variable orifice where it enters the bottom chamber. However, the pressure drops calculated here do not include the pressure losses across these orifices. The dimensions of the sleeves are given in Fig. 42.

The lower end of the safety rod will be 1 in. above the top of the core lattice when the safety rod is in the "full out" position. The safety rod dimensions are given in Fig. 56, and the results of the calculations are given in Fig. 57.

# DIVISION OF FLOW OF HELIUM

The flow of the helium to the various holes is apportioned so that the minimum flow to the instrument in the "full in" position is 0.005 lb/sec, and the minimum flow to the regulating rod in the "full out" position is 0.034 lb/sec. The flow rates per hole for all combinations of regulating rod and instrument positions, with the safety rod out of the core, are given in Table ll.

# PERFORMANCE OF ROD AND INSTRUMENT COOLING SYSTEM HEAT EXCHANGER

The control rods and nuclear instruments in the core of the ARE are cooled by helium, which gives up its heat in a helium-to-water exchanger (Fig. 60). The equations governing the performance of this heat exchanger are

$$
\begin{array}{l} Q = W _ {\mathrm {H e}} c _ {p _ {\mathrm {H e}}} \left(T _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {\prime} - T _ {\mathrm {H e}}\right), (29) \\ Q = W _ {w} c _ {p _ {w}} \left(T _ {w} ^ {\prime} - T _ {w}\right), (30) \\ Q = h A \theta , (31) \\ \end{array}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} Q = \text {h e a t} \quad \text {t r a n s f e r r e d}, \quad \text {B t u / s e c}, \\ W = \text {w e i g h t f l o w}, \quad \mathrm {l b / s e c}, \\ c _ {p} = \text {s p e c i f i c h e a t}, \mathrm {B t u / l b} ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}, \\ T ^ {\prime} = \text {m a x i m u m t e m p e r a t u r e ,} ^ {\circ} F, \\ T = \text {m i n i m u m t e m p e r a t u r e}, ^ {\circ} F, \\ \end{array}
$$

$h =$ over-all heat transfer coefficient, Btu/sec·ft²·°F,

$$
\begin{array}{l} A = \text {h e a t} \quad \text {t r a n s f e r} \quad \text {a r e a}, \quad \mathrm {f t} ^ {2}, \\ \theta = \text {e f f e c t i v e} \log \text {m e a n} \text {t e m p e r a t u r e} \text {d i f f e r e n c e}. \\ \end{array}
$$

The heat exchanger operates under the following restraints and conditions:

1. The helium flow is 1000 cfm at blower temperature.

![](images/621b013c78597c2dd35f453ba9b4e218774392baff0f65b24a1f24f171300317.jpg)  
Fig. 59. Pressure Drop in Instrument Sleeve.

TABLE 11. HELIUM FLOW RATES IN ROD AND INSTRUMENT HOLES

![](images/6840dd481b5f963cecbab12d9ddf4a628d32ad3b59c53682bd716cbadafb8f38.jpg)  
Fig. 60. Helium-to-Water Heat Exchanger.

2. The water outlet temperature is thermostatically controlled and is held fixed at $100^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

(32) $hA = \left[\frac{1}{(hA)_{\text{H}}}\right] + \frac{1}{(hA)_{\text{m}}} + \frac{1}{(hA)_{\text{w}}}\bigg]^{-1}$ ;

$$
h _ {\mathrm {H e}} = 1. 8 6 \frac {k _ {\mathrm {H e}}}{D _ {\mathrm {H e}}} \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {0. 1 4} \operatorname {R e} _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {1 / 3} \Pr_ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {1 / 3} \left(\frac {D _ {\mathrm {H e}}}{L}\right) ^ {1 / 3} \tag {33}
$$

3. The water inlet temperature will vary with the season of the year, but has been assumed to be $70^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ for this calculation.

The rod cooling system heat exchanger consists of three standard shell-and-tube heat exchangers arranged in parallel. The helium flows in the tubes, and the water flows across the outside of the tubes. The following are pertinent physical dimensions of the exchanger; all values are for one of three units:

Shell inside diameter, in. 61/8

Tube length, ft 3

Tube outside diameter, in. 0.375

Tube inside diameter, in. 0.319

Number of tubes 116

Surface area per tube (inside), ft² 0.251

Surface area per tube (outside), ft² 0.295

Surface area (inside), ft² 29.1

Surface area (outside), ft² 34.2

Baffle spacing, in. 2

Tube pitch (triangular pattern), in. 29/64

The heat transfer coefficient may be evaluated from the following equations:

(McAdams, p. 190 of ref. 11), where the calculation indicates that the helium flow is laminar;

$$
h _ {w} = \frac {k _ {w}}{D _ {w}} \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) _ {w} ^ {0. 1 4} \quad \mathrm {R e} _ {w} ^ {0. 5 5} \quad \Pr_ {w} ^ {1 / 3} \tag {34}
$$

(Kern, p. 137 of ref. 8), where

$$
\mathrm {R e} _ {w} = \frac {G _ {w} D _ {w}}{\mu_ {w}},
$$

$$
D _ {w} = \frac {4 \times \text {f r e e a r e a}}{\text {w e t t e d p e r i m e t e r}} (\text {f r e e a r e a}
$$

measured in a plane perpendicular to the tubes),

$$
G _ {w} = \frac {d C B}{p};
$$

$$
\left(\frac {1}{h A}\right) _ {m} = \frac {2 \pi k _ {m} L}{\ln \frac {D _ {\mathrm {H e}}}{D _ {v}}} \tag {35}
$$

where $k =$ thermal conductivity, Btu/sec $\cdot \mathrm{ft}^2$ (°F/ft),

$D =$ equivalent diameter, ft,

$\mu =$ viscosity at bulk temperature, lb/hr·ft,

$\mu_{s} = \text{viscosity at surface temperature},$ $\mathrm{lb / hr}\cdot \mathrm{ft}$

Re = Reynolds' number,

$\mathrm{Pr} = \mathrm{Prand}t1's$ number,

L = tube length, ft,

$G =$ unit weight flow, $1\mathrm{b / hr}\cdot \mathrm{ft}^2$

$d =$ shell inside diameter, ft,

$C =$ tube clearance, ft,

$B =$ distance between baffles, ft,

$p =$ tube pitch, ft,

and the subscripts

He = heli um,

$w = \text{water}$ .

The helium and water heat transfer areas are tabulated above: 29.1 and 34.2 ft² per one unit of three, respectively.

By using Eqs. 29 through 35 and noting that the large number of baffles on the shell side makes the effective log mean temperature difference almost equal to the log mean temperature difference for counterflow, the performance of the rod cooling system heat exchanger can be calculated. Figure 61 shows the result of such a calculation; the helium inlet and outlet temperatures are plotted against the heat transferred. Some of the pertinent information not shown in Fig. 60 is tabulated in Table 12.

The heat removed from the core in the rod cooling circuit, calculated in the first section of this chapter, is about 25 Btu/sec. From Fig. 61, it may be seen that the minimum and maximum temperatures in the rod cooling heat exchanger will be about 110 and $240^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , respectively. The helium will actually pick up a considerable amount of heat between the heat exchanger and the inlet to the reactor and, consequently, the inlet and outlet temperatures of the heat exchangers will be greater than those listed above. The inlet temperature to the reactor will be about $150^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

![](images/7f0b1d6cf76d202bee72cef7ad0a91985e5cf937860361f9222af732fcac2098.jpg)  
Fig. 61. Helium Inlet and Outlet Temperature vs. Heat Transferred.

# Chapter 6

# MONITORING AND PREHEAT SYSTEM

# HEAT LOSS THROUGH INSULATION

The heat loss to the environment of the various pieces of equipment in the three ARE pits is complicated by the fact that the pits are filled with helium. The helium tends to replace the air in the pores of the insulation and thus to increase the thermal conductivity of the insulation. The following formula for the thermal conductivity of porous insulating materials was derived by Maxwell and reported by Jakob (cf., p. 85 of ref. 14):

$$
k = k _ {s} \frac {1 - \left(1 - \frac {a k g}{k _ {s}}\right) b}{1 + (a - 1) b}, \tag {1}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} a = \frac {3 k _ {s}}{2 k _ {s} + k _ {g}}, \\ b = \frac {V _ {\mathrm {g}}}{V _ {\mathrm {s}} + V _ {\mathrm {g}}} = 1 - \frac {V _ {\mathrm {s}}}{V _ {\mathrm {s}} + V _ {\mathrm {g}}} = 1 - \frac {\rho}{\rho_ {\mathrm {s}}} \\ \begin{array}{l} k = \text {t h e r m a l c o n d u c t i v i t y , B t u / h r} ^ {\cdot} f t ^ {2} \\ (^ {\circ} F / f t), \end{array} \\ V = \text {v o l u m e}, f t ^ {3}, \\ \rho = \text {d e n s i t y}, \quad \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}, \\ \end{array}
$$

and the subscripts

$$
\begin{array}{l} s = \text {s o l i d}, \\ g = g a s. \\ \end{array}
$$

The ARE uses three types of insulation; diatomaceous earth and Superex for high-temperature insulation, and felted Asbestos-Sponge for lower-temperature insulation. The properties of these insulations are given in Table 13. By using the data of Table 13 and mean thermal conductivities of 0.033 and 0.021 Btu/hr·ft² (°F/ft) for the air in the high- and low-temperature insulation, respectively, the thermal conductivity of the solid, $k_{s}$ , can be determined from Eq. 1. Since $k_{s}$ is known, $k$ can be calculated for the case with helium in the pores by using 0.15 and 0.11 Btu/hr·ft² (°F/ft),

respectively, for the high- and low-temperature mean thermal conductivities of helium. The results of the calculations are tabulated in Table 14.

The insulation on the outside of the ARE piping and equipment is a combination of Superex and Asbestos-Sponge, and diatomaceous earth is used for insulation in the control rod cooling loop. The heat loss to the environment through the combination Superex and Asbestos-Sponge insulation may be calculated from the following equations:

1. For insulating cylindrical shapes (i.e., piping),

$$
\begin{array}{l} Q = \frac {2 \pi k _ {s} \theta_ {s}}{\ln \frac {D _ {s}}{D _ {s} ^ {\prime}}} (2) \\ Q = \frac {2 \pi k _ {a - s} \theta_ {a - s}}{\ln \frac {D _ {a - s}}{D _ {a - s} ^ {\prime}}} , (3) \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} Q = \frac {0 . 1 7 3}{3 6 0 0} \in A \left[ \left(\frac {T _ {s}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} - \left(\frac {T _ {e}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} \right] \tag {4} \\ + h _ {c} A \left(T _ {s} - T _ {e}\right), \\ \end{array}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \begin{array}{c} Q = \text {h e a t l o s s p e r l i n e a l f o o t}, \\ \text {B t u / s e c} \cdot \text {l i n e a l f t}, \end{array} \\ \begin{array}{l} k = \text {t h e r m a l c o n d u c t i v i t y , B t u / s e c} \cdot f t ^ {2} \\ \left(^ {\circ} F / f t\right), \end{array} \\ \theta = \text {t e m p e r a t u r e} ^ {\circ} \mathbf {F}, \\ D = \text {o u t s i d e} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} D ^ {\prime} = \text {i n s i d e} \\ \epsilon = \text {e m i s s i v i t y} \\ \begin{array}{l} A = \text {o u t s i d e s u r f a c e a r e a p e r l i n e a l} \\ \text {f o o t , f t ^ {2} / l i n e a l f t ,} \end{array} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} T _ {s} = \begin{array}{l} \text {t e m p e r a t u r e o f o u t s i d e s u r f a c e ,} \\ \mathrm {R}, \end{array} \\ \begin{array}{l} T _ {e} = \text {t e m p e r a t u r e o f e n v i r o n m e n t ,} ^ {\circ} R, \\ h _ {c} = \text {f r e e c o n v e c t i o n h e a t t r a n s f e r} \\ \text {c o e f f i c i e n t , B t u / s e c} \cdot \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} \cdot {} ^ {\circ} F, \end{array} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} a n d t h e s u b s c r i p t s \\ s = \text {S u p e r e x}, \\ a - s = \text {A s b e s t o s - S p o n g e}. \\ \end{array}
$$

TABLE 13. PROPERTIES OF VARIOUS INSULATING MATERIALS

The free convection heat transfer coefficient is determined from the data of Nusselt, $^{(18)}$ McAdams, and King, $^{(19)}$ who correlated Nusselt's number with Grashof's and Prandtl's numbers (as given by Jakob $^{(14)}$ ).

2. For insulating plane shapes, Eqs. 2, 3, and 4 become

(5) $q = \frac{k_s}{t_s} \theta_s$ ,

(6) $q = \frac{k_{a - s}}{t_{a - s}}\theta_s$

(7) $q = \frac{0.173}{3600} \in \left[\left(\frac{T_s}{100}\right)^4 - \left(\frac{T_e}{100}\right)^4\right]$

$$
+ h _ {c} \left(T _ {s} - T _ {e}\right),
$$

where

$q =$ heat loss per unit area, Btu/sec·ft²,

$t =$ thickness, ft.

The heat loss per lineal foot of the piping was calculated by using Eqs. 2, 3, and 4. The dimensions of

the pipe, annulus, heaters, and insulation of the fuel and NaK piping are given in the following:
