# REACTOR PREHEATING

The reactor of the ARE, with its large mass of beryllium oxide, has the largest heat capacity of any piece of equipment in the ARE. The reactor must be raised to a high temperature before the system can be filled with fuel carrier, since the fuel carrier has a melting point of approximately $950^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . Figures 9 and 12 show elevation and plan views of the reactor; Fig. 9 shows the reactor and its thermal insulation, which is attached to a metal shell that is separate from the reactor. The heat is supplied by electrical strip heaters strapped to the reactor itself. The procedure for preheating the reactor will be the following:

1. turn the heaters on at a power of $10 \, \text{kw}$ ;   
2. increase the heater power as the pressure shell temperature in-

![](images/0be89c2adfa8893eefd6039bf046ff16ac2ae328c0d024133bb37054e6a63cff.jpg)  
Fig. 62. Heat Loss Through Insulation.

creases so that the heater power is equal to 10 kw plus the heat loss through the insulation;

3. when the pressure shell temperature reaches $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , gradually reduce the heater power to hold the pressure shell temperature at $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

When no further changes in heater power are required to hold the pressure shell at $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , equilibrium will have been attained and the preheat process completed.

Steps 1 and 2 in the preheat process imply that a constant amount of heat is being added to the reactor, namely, $10\mathrm{kw}$ . This method of preheating will

TABLE 16. HEAT LOSSES IN THE ITEMS IN THE ARE PITS   

<table><tr><td>ITEM</td><td>LOCATION</td><td>HEAT LOSS (kw)</td></tr><tr><td>Reactor and reactor thermal insulation</td><td>Reactor pit</td><td>15</td></tr><tr><td>Fuel system heat exchangers</td><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><td>Fuel system surge tanks</td><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>4</td></tr><tr><td>Fuel pumps</td><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td>Reflector coolant system heat exchangers</td><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td>Reflector coolant surge tanks</td><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>4</td></tr><tr><td>Reflector coolant pumps</td><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td>Fill and flush tanks</td><td>Dump tank pit</td><td>25</td></tr><tr><td>Fuel dump tank</td><td>Dump tank pit</td><td>8</td></tr><tr><td>Fuel surge tank vapor traps</td><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td>Fuel dump tank vapor trap</td><td>Dump tank pit</td><td>2</td></tr><tr><td>NaK purification system</td><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>10</td></tr><tr><td>Valves</td><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>15</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Dump tank pit</td><td>12</td></tr><tr><td>Piping</td><td>Reactor pit</td><td>15</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>85</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Dump tank pit</td><td>30</td></tr></table>

result in a quasi-stationary temperature state being set up in the reactor after some initial period. A quasi-stationary temperature means that the temperature difference between any two points remains constant even though the entire temperature level is rising. The preheating process can therefore be broken up into three periods:

1. the initial period before the quasi-stationary state is obtained,   
2. the period when the reactor is in the quasi-stationary temperature state,   
3. the period after the pressure shell has reached $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , during which the heater power is being gradually reduced and the quasi-stationary state no longer exists.

The following assumptions were made for the calculation of the reactor preheating:

1. The reactor will be represented by six concentric beryllium oxide

shells, 3 ft long and 3.75 in. thick, separated by helium shells 0.050 in. wide.

2. The pressure shell will be constructed of Inconel and will be 48 in. in inside diameter and 2 in. thick.   
3. There will be a 1/16 in, helium gap between the pressure shell and outer beryllium oxide cylinder.   
4. The heat loss from the ends of the reactor will be considered, but longitudinal heat flow within the reactor will be neglected.

The following physical properties, which represent mean values between 100 and $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , were used in the analysis:

<table><tr><td></td><td>BeO</td><td>INCONEL</td><td>HELIUM</td></tr><tr><td>Thermal conductivity, Btu/hr·ft2(°F/ft)</td><td>10</td><td>12</td><td>0.13</td></tr><tr><td>Density, lb/ft3</td><td>184</td><td>518</td><td>0.0055</td></tr><tr><td>Specific heat, Btu/lb·°F</td><td>0.38</td><td>0.15</td><td>1.24</td></tr></table>

![](images/82d31fc1425e6324bd53ac12bbb9cdc34aa229de55b93d5fc4a2e5aadca09749.jpg)  
Fig. 63. Composite Plan View of Piping Inside Shield.

![](images/de2b992cc37258873e9cef7cba0ba952959b584c1c65e86043572e9f0af6ad5b.jpg)

The temperature difference between any radial station in the reactor and the outside of the pressure shell when the reactor is in the quasi-stationary temperature state can be calculated from the following equation:

$$
\frac {d \theta}{d t} = \frac {Q}{\left(W _ {C p}\right) _ {\text {B e O}} + \left(W _ {C p}\right) _ {\text {I n c}} + \left(W _ {C p}\right) _ {\text {H e}}} ， \tag {8}
$$

where

$$
\frac {d \theta}{d t} = \begin{array}{l} \text {r a t e o f t e m p e r a t u r e r i s e ,} \\ \text {° F / h r , o f a n y p o i n t i n t h e} \\ \text {r e a c t o r (i n t h e q u a s i - s t a n a t o r y} \\ \text {t e m p e r a t u r e s t a t e) ,} \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{c} Q = \text {h e a t e r i n p u t t o t h e r e a c t o r ,} \\ \text {B t u / h r ,} \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} W = \text {w e i g h t o f m a t e r i a l i n t h e} \\ \text {r e a c t o r , l b ,} \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} c _ {p} = \text {s p e c i f i c h e a t , B t u / l b} ^ {\circ} F, \\ \text {a n d t h e s u b s c r i p t s} \end{array}
$$

$$
\mathrm {B e O} = \text {b e r y l l i u m o x i d e},
$$

$$
I n c = I n c o n e l,
$$

$$
\mathrm {H e} = \text {h e l i u m}.
$$

The weight of the beryllium oxide in the reactor is approximately 5800 lb; the Inconel in the pressure shell weighs about 7900 lb; the helium weight can be neglected. From Eq. 8,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {d \theta}{d t} = \frac {3 4 , 1 0 0}{5 8 0 0 \times 0 . 3 8 + 7 9 0 0 \times 0 . 1 5} \\ = 1 0. 1 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F} / \mathrm {h r}. \\ \end{array}
$$

The temperature difference across any of the cylindrical shells may be approximated in the following manner:

$$
\Delta \theta_ {n} = \frac {q _ {n} \ln \frac {r _ {n + 1}}{r _ {n}}}{2 \pi k _ {n} L}, \tag {9}
$$

$$
q _ {n} = 3 4, 1 0 0 - \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {n - 1} \left(W _ {c _ {p}}\right) _ {i} \frac {d \theta}{d t}, \tag {10}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \begin{array}{l} \triangle \theta = \text {r a d i a l t e m p e r a t u r e d i f f e r e n t i a l} \\ \text {a c r o s s a s h e l l (i n t h e q u a s i - s t a n i o n a r y t e m p e r a t u r e s t a t e) ,} \\ \quad^ {\circ} F, \end{array} \\ q = \text {h e a t e n t e r i n g s h e l l}, \mathrm {B t u / h r}, \\ r = \text {o u t s i d e} r a d i u s o f \text {s h e l l}, \quad \text {f t}, \\ \begin{array}{l} k = \text {t h e r m a l c o n d u c t i v i t y o f s h e l l}, \\ B t u / h r \cdot f t ^ {2} (° F / f t), \end{array} \\ L = \text {l e n g t h o f s h e l l}, \quad \mathrm {f t}, \\ \end{array}
$$

and the subscript $n$ refers to the number of the shells, counting inward from the outside. (For a more accurate estimate, the fact that all the $q_{n}$ in Eq. 10 is not transferred across shell $n$ should be considered. The above approach is a conservative simplification, however, because it leads to larger values of $\Delta \theta_{n}$ .)

The results of the solution of Eqs. 9 and 10 are shown in Fig. 64, which gives the difference in temperature (in the quasi-stationary state) between the outside of the pressure shell and any radial position. The difference between the outside of the pressure shell and the center of the reactor is $162^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

The time required to reach the quasi-stationary temperature state may be readily calculated. The reactor is originally at some uniform temperature $\theta_0$ . The quasi-stationary state is reached while the temperature of the center of the reactor remains fixed at $\theta_0$ and the outer shells heat up to a temperature of

$$
\theta_ {n _ {1}} = \theta_ {0} + \sum_ {i = n + 1} ^ {N} \Delta \theta_ {i} + \frac {1}{2} \Delta \theta_ {n}, \tag {11}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \theta_ {n _ {1}} = \text {t e m p e r a t u r e o f t h e n t h s h e l l a t} \\ \text {t h e b e g i n n i n g o f t h e q u a s i -} \\ \text {s t a t i o n a r y s t a t e ,} \\ N = \text {t o t a l n u m b e r o f s h e l l s}. \\ \end{array}
$$

The time required to reach the quasi-stationary temperature state is,

![](images/d7f6b5692a2c28b33c88536f6ad5ea621a0c62df1bec1855b56a25828955dea5.jpg)  
Fig. 64. Temperature Profile of Reactor Core and Pressure Shell (Quasi-Stationary State).

therefore,

$$
t _ {1} = \frac {\sum_ {i = 1} ^ {N} \left(W c _ {p}\right) _ {i} \left(\theta_ {i _ {1}} - \theta_ {0}\right)}{Q}, \tag {12}
$$

where $t_1$ is the time required to reach the quasi-stationary temperature state in hours. This time was calculated from Eq. 12 to be about 10.0 hours. Thus, after about 10 hr, the temperature of the outside of the pressure shell will be at about $162^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ above ambient temperature, while the center of the core will still be at about ambient temperature. The actual ambient temperature in the pits will probably vary during the preheating period; most of the calculations of this report assume an ambient temperature of $130^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ in the pit. It was assumed for these calculations that the reactor and pit were both, at the

until the reactor pressure shell outer temperature reaches $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . During this period, the temperatures are in the quasi-stationary state, and every temperature in the reactor is raised uniformly at the rate of $10.1^{\circ}\mathrm{F/hr}$ :

$$
t _ {2} - t _ {1} = \frac {1 2 0 0 - 2 9 2}{1 0 . 1} = 8 9. 9 \mathrm {h r}, \tag {13}
$$

where $t_2$ is the time at the end of the quasi-stationary state. During this period, the electrical heater power must be continually increased as the pressure shell temperature increases to maintain a constant rate of heat flow into the reactor. The heater power must therefore be equal to 10 kw plus the heat loss through the insulation. The heat loss through the insulation may be calculated as a function of reactor pressure shell outer temperature from the following equations:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \left. \quad q = h _ {1} A _ {1} \left(T _ {1} - T _ {\mathrm {H e}}\right) + \frac {0 . 1 7 3}{3 6 0 0} \frac {A _ {1}}{\frac {1}{\epsilon_ {1}} + \frac {A _ {1}}{A _ {2}} \left(\frac {1}{\epsilon_ {2}} - 1\right)} \left[ \left(\frac {T _ {1}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} - \left(\frac {T _ {2}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} \right] \right., \\ q = h _ {2} A _ {2} \left(T _ {\mathrm {H e}} - T _ {2}\right) + \frac {0 . 1 7 3}{3 6 0 0} \frac {A _ {1}}{\frac {1}{\epsilon_ {1}} + \frac {A _ {1}}{A _ {2}} \left(\frac {1}{\epsilon_ {2}} - 1\right)} \left[ \left(\frac {T _ {1}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} - \left(\frac {T _ {2}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} \right], (15) \\ q = \frac {k _ {I}}{\tau_ {I}} A _ {I} \left(T _ {2} - T _ {3}\right), (16) \\ q = h _ {3} A _ {3} \left(T _ {3} - T _ {a}\right) + \frac {0 . 1 7 3}{3 6 0 0}: \epsilon_ {3} A _ {3} \left[ \left(\frac {T _ {3}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} - \left(\frac {T _ {a}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} \right], (17) \\ \end{array}
$$

start, at a temperature of $130^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . The time required to raise the mean temperature of the reactor from 70 to $130^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ is about 6 hours. It may therefore be considered that $t_1$ is really about 16 hr and that at the end of the 16-hr period the reactor pressure shell outer temperature will be about $292^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ and the center-line temperature will be about $130^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

The next period of the preheating process is the heating of the reactor

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \begin{array}{l} q = \text {h e a t l o s s t h r o u g h i n s u l a t i o n ,} \\ \text {B t u / s e c ,} \end{array} \\ \begin{array}{l} h = \text {h e a t t r a n s f e r c o e f f i c i e n t}, \\ \text {B t u / s e c} \cdot \text {f t} ^ {2} \cdot {} ^ {\circ} \text {F}, \end{array} \\ A = \text {h e a t} \quad \text {t r a n s f e r} \quad \text {a r e a}, \quad \mathrm {f t} ^ {2}, \\ T = \text {t e m p e r a t u r e}, ^ {\circ} R, \\ \begin{array}{l} k = \text {t h e r m a l c o n d u c t i v i t y , B t u / s e c} \cdot f t ^ {2} \\ (\circ F / f t), \end{array} \\ \tau = \text {t h i c k n e s s}, f t, \\ \epsilon = \text {e m i s s i v i t y}, \\ \end{array}
$$

and the subscripts

1 = outside of reactor pressure shell,   
2 = inside of thermal insulation,   
$3 =$ outside of thermal insulation,   
$I =$ insulation,   
He = helium between pressure shell and thermal insulation,   
$\pmb{a} =$ ambient.

Equations 14 through 17 and the following data were used to calculate the heat loss through the insulation for various pressure shell temperatures:

1. $\epsilon = 1.0$   
2. The data of King(19) were used to evaluate $h_1$ .   
3. The dimensions of thermal insulation were:

Outside diameter 76.25 in.

Outside length 80.5 in.

Thickness 6 in. (4 in. of Superex, 2 in. of Asbestos-Sponge)

4. The thermal conductivity value for the insulation, as found in the previous section on "Heat Loss Through Insulation," was used.

The results of the calculation are shown in Fig. 65, where the heat loss through the insulation is plotted against the reactor pressure shell outer temperature. Temperatures at other points in the reactor thermal insulation are shown by the dotted line.

The last portion of the preheating process, that is, when the heater power is reduced to maintain the pressure shell outer temperature at $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , is evaluated by a graphical method developed by Schmidt.(20) As a simplification, each helium shell is divided between adjacent beryllium oxide shells, and the Inconel pressure shell is replaced by an equivalent thickness of beryllium oxide. The reactor is then treated as a homogeneous cylinder with a thermal conductivity equal to the average thermal conductivity of the composite beryllium oxide and helium shells. A plot of the temperatures in the reactor for the first 8 hr of the last portion of the preheating period is shown in Fig. 66.

![](images/533f92db5472bf0a5d96694bf4f4080ca74f3dcc31711335bc5db00d75bff59e.jpg)  
Fig. 65. Temperature of Outer Side of Pressure Shell vs. Heat Loss Through Insulation.

After 8 hr, the temperature of the core center line is about $1125^{\circ} \mathrm{F}$ .

A summary of the temperatures and powers in the entire preheating period is shown in Fig. 67.

The maximum temperature gradient in the pressure shell at any time is approximately $6^{\circ}\mathrm{F}/\mathrm{inch}$ .

# HELIUM LEAKAGE THROUGH CLEARANCE HOLES IN THE REACTOR THERMAL SHIELD

The tubes which contain the control rods and fission chambers pierce the reactor thermal insulation at both the top and bottom, and there are six such tubes. Figure 68 shows a sketch of a typical tube and the passage through the reactor thermal shield. The helium inside the reactor thermal shield, being at a higher temperature than the room helium, will flow through the clearance holes in the reactor thermal insulation and consequently will add

![](images/64ca0c725757cb05415852e94ac1bbdd23fdb804c167882d09dc40832ea1f163.jpg)  
Fig. 66. Temperature vs. Time Relationship for Reactor Core and Pressure Shell.

to the room cooling requirements and the reactor electric heater requirements.

It was assumed that the flow through the bottom clearance holes was negligible and that the flow through the upper clearance holes was that due to the difference in head between the helium in the clearance holes and room helium. The head available for flow is, then,

$$
P = h \left(\rho_ {a} - \rho_ {c}\right), \tag {18}
$$

where

$$
P = \text {p r e s s u r e} \quad \text {h e a d}, \quad \mathrm {l b / f t} ^ {2},
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \rho_ {a} = \text {d e n s i t y o f a m b i e n t h e l i u m}, \\ \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \quad \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \rho_ {c} = \text {d e n s i t y o f h e l i u m i n c l e a r a n c e} \\ \text {h o l e , l b / f t} ^ {3}, \end{array}
$$

$h =$ height of clearance hole, ft. To be conservative, it was assumed that the temperature of the helium in the

clearance hole was $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ ; the room temperature was taken as about $130^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . Thus

$$
\begin{array}{l} P = \frac {6 . 1 2 5}{1 2} \times (0. 0 0 9 2 - 0. 0 0 3 3 5) \\ = 0. 0 0 2 9 9 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {f t} ^ {2}. \\ \end{array}
$$

The loss in pressure head in a gas flowing through an annulus in laminar flow is

$$
P = \frac {1 2 \mu G L}{y ^ {2} g _ {c} \rho}, \tag {19}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mu = \text {v i s c o s i t y}, \quad \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {h r} \cdot \mathrm {f t}, \\ G = \text {f l o w}, \quad \mathrm {l b / h r} \cdot \mathrm {f t} ^ {2}, \\ \begin{array}{l} L = \text {l e n g t h} = \text {h e i g h t o f c l e a r a n c e} \\ \text {h o l e , f t ,} \end{array} \\ y = \text {a n n u l u s} \quad \text {g a p}, \quad \text {f t}, \\ g _ {c} = \text {g r a v i t a t i o n a l c o n s t a n t}, f t / h r ^ {2}, \\ \rho = \text {g a s} \quad \text {d e n s i t y}, \quad \mathrm {l b / f t} ^ {3}. \\ \end{array}
$$

![](images/03e43db4cef74dd181251659caddf24353d31db258462ed2ed3cfebb220f824c.jpg)  
Fig. 67. Summary of Temperature and Power in Entire Preheating Period.

An evaluation of Eq. 19 gives

$$
P = \frac {1 2 \times 0 . 1 0 2 \times G \times \frac {6 . 1 2 5}{1 2}}{\left(\frac {0 . 3 9}{1 2}\right) ^ {2} \times 3 2 . 2 \times (3 6 0 0) ^ {2} \times (0 . 0 0 3 3 5)} = 4. 2 4 \times 1 0 ^ {- 4} G l b / f t ^ {2}.
$$

The flow, $G$ , is then evaluated by equating the available pressure head to the loss in head because of flow:

$$
G = 7. 0 5 \mathrm {l b} / \mathrm {h r} \cdot \mathrm {f t} ^ {2}.
$$

Since there are six identical clearance holes, the total flow area is

$$
\begin{array}{l} A = 6 \times \frac {\pi}{4} \left[ \left(\frac {3 . 7 5}{1 2}\right) ^ {2} - \left(\frac {2 . 9 7}{1 2}\right) ^ {2} \right] \\ = 0. 1 7 1 5 f t ^ {2}. \\ \end{array}
$$

Therefore the weight flow is

$$
W = 0. 1 7 1 5 \times 7. 0 5 = 1. 2 1 \mathrm {l b / h r},
$$

![](images/fe64108287c61d2daa19678ca3c19942f83371cd8ae4c9b0d481cc3d17fba654.jpg)  
Fig. 68. Rod Sleeve Through Thermal Shield.

and the heat loss is

$$
\begin{array}{l} Q = W c _ {p} \left(T _ {c} - T _ {a}\right) = 1. 2 1 \times 1. 2 4 (1 2 0 0 - 1 3 0) \\ = 1 6 0 5 \mathrm {B t u / h r} \\ = 0. 4 4 6 \mathrm {B t u} / \sec , \\ \end{array}
$$

where

$T_{c} =$ temperature in clearance hole, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{F}$ $T_{a} =$ ambient temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{F}$

The heat loss through the clearance holes is therefore approximately 0.45 Btu/sec.

# SPACE COOLER PERFORMANCE

The bulk of the equipment of the ARE is contained in three sealed pits,

which are kept cool by the use of space coolers. A typical space cooler is shown in Fig. 69. Each space cooler contains two helium blowers in parallel (total capacity $7200\mathrm{cfm}$ ) and a water cooling coil. The entire unit was supplied by the Trane Co. The cooling coil is a Trane Co., series 93, type S coil, with a 12- by 48-in. face and four rows of tubes longitudinal to the gas flow. The heat transfer coefficient of such a coil, operating with air on one side and water on the other, is given in ref. 21, for various water and air velocities. As used in the ARE, these coils will have helium instead of air on the gas side.

Since it was necessary to convert the performance data from those for air-to-water operation to those for helium-to-water operation, the following equation, from which values can be obtained that are approximately the same as the actual performance data given in ref. 21, was used:

$$
\frac {1}{h A} = \frac {0 . 0 0 3 7 4}{v _ {w} ^ {0 . 8}} + \frac {0 . 1 8 9}{v _ {a} ^ {0 . 6}}, \tag {20}
$$

where

$h =$ over-all heat transfer coefficient, Btu/hr·ft²·°F,   
$A =$ heat transfer area per row per square foot of coil face area, ft²,   
$\nu_{w} = \text{water velocity, ft/sec}$ ,   
$\nu_{a} = \text{air velocity, ft/min.}$

To use Eq. 20, an equivalent air

![](images/67272fdc733a8e9f0ee276e41aa5c16d79e1fa695c45462934fc47600f54b3f2.jpg)  
Fig. 69. Space Cooler.

velocity, that is, the air velocity which would give the same heat transfer coefficient as the actual helium velocity, had to be calculated. Since the fin efficiency of the coil fins is very close to $100\%$ at all times (sheet fins of aluminum, 0.012 in. thick, with the tubes on a triangular pitch of approximately 1.2 in.), it may be assumed that the gas-side heat transfer coefficient is a function of the Reynolds' number to the 0.6 power. Since the Prandtl's numbers for air and for helium are about the same at this temperature and since the geometry of the coil is unchanged, the equivalent air velocity can be calculated from the following equation:

$$
\frac {h _ {a}}{h _ {\mathrm {H e}}} = 1. 0 = \frac {k _ {a} v _ {a} ^ {0 . 6} \rho_ {a} ^ {0 . 6} \mu_ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {0 . 6}}{k _ {\mathrm {H e}} v _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {0 . 6} \rho_ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {0 . 6} \mu_ {a} ^ {0 . 6}} \tag {21}
$$

or

$$
\frac {v _ {a}}{v _ {\mathrm {H e}}} = \left(\frac {k _ {\mathrm {H e}}}{k _ {a}}\right) ^ {1. 6 6 7} \frac {\rho_ {\mathrm {H e}}}{\rho_ {a}} \frac {\mu_ {a}}{\mu_ {\mathrm {H e}}}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} k = \text {t h e r m a l} \\ \rho = \text {d e n s i t y}, \\ \mu = \text {v i s c o s i t y}, \\ h = \text {h e a t} \\ a = \text {e q u i v a l e n t} \\ v _ {\mathrm {H e}} = \text {h e l i u m v e l o c i t y}. \\ \end{array}
$$

Since the equivalent air velocity (calculated above) and the water flow are known, the over-all $hA$ can be calculated from Eq. 20.

The equations governing the operation of the space coolers are

$$
\begin{array}{l} Q = W _ {H e} c _ {p _ {H e}} \left(T _ {H e} ^ {\prime} - T _ {H e}\right), (22) \\ Q = W _ {w} c _ {p _ {w}} \left(T _ {w} ^ {\prime} - T _ {w}\right), (23) \\ Q = h A \theta , (24) \\ \end{array}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} Q = \text {h e a t} \quad \text {t r a n s f e r r e d}, \quad \text {B t u / s e c}, \\ \begin{array}{l} \theta = \log \text {m e a n t e m p e r a t u r e d i f f e r e n c e}, \\ \quad^ {\circ} F, \end{array} \\ W = \text {w e i g h t f l o w}; \quad 1 b / \sec , \\ c _ {p} = \text {s p e c i f i c h e a t}, \mathrm {B t u / l b} ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}, \\ T ^ {\prime} = \text {m a x i m u m t e m p e r a t u r e (a l s o e q u a l s} \quad \text {a m b i e n t t e m p e r a t u r e) ,} \quad {} ^ {\circ} F, \\ T = \text {m i n i m u m t e m p e r a t u r e ,} ^ {\circ} F, \\ \end{array}
$$

and the subscript $w$ refers to water. The system operates under the following restraints and conditions:

1. The helium flow is 7200 cfm at blower temperature.   
2. The outlet water temperature is thermostatically controlled to be $100^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .   
3. The inlet water temperature varies with the season of the year but is assumed to be $70^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ for this calculation.

Equations 20 through 24 and the restraints and condition listed above can be used to evaluate the performance of the space coolers. Figure 70 shows the variation of heat transferred vs. ambient temperature for one of the space coolers. (The heat exchanger pit contains four such units; the other pits each contain two units.) The difference between the maximum helium temperature (ambient) and the minimum helium temperature is small; that is, it varies from about 0.2 deg at a heat load of 25 Btu/sec to about 1.6 deg at a heat load of 200 Btu/sec.

The heat rejected to the ambient atmosphere in the three ARE pits from the various items of equipment in the pits, calculated in the previous section on "Heat Loss Through Insulation," is tabulated in the following:

<table><tr><td>Reactor pit</td><td>30 kw</td></tr><tr><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>135</td></tr><tr><td>Dump tank pit</td><td>75</td></tr><tr><td>Total</td><td>240 kw</td></tr></table>

The three pits are interconnected by passages of relatively small flow area. Therefore the pits will not operate at the same temperature, nor will they operate at the temperatures which would exist if the pits were completely sealed from one another. The pit operating temperatures were calculated for the two extreme cases (pits at the same temperature; pits completely independent) by using the data of Fig. 70, which gives the space cooler performance. It was assumed that the heat loss from the pits to the outside through the concrete walls could be neglected; this is a conservative assumption. Table 17 gives the heat losses in the pits.

![](images/3f8652aee5e3de16c77f8121155d4d8fe1c79bb337b81f7dcffefd60ace9bf17.jpg)  
Fig. 70. Space Cooler Performance.

The pits will therefore operate at a temperature of about 110 to $120^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . It should be noted that all the heat loss calculations in the section on "Heat Loss Through Insulation" were made with an assumed ambient temperature of $130^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . Therefore the heat losses listed in Table 17 are slightly low. The difference is quite small, however, inasmuch as the surface temperature of most of the equipment losing heat is from 1150 to $1500^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

# TEMPERATURE PATTERNS IN THE MONITORING ANNULUS IN THE EVENT OF HEAT FAILURE

The fuel and reflector coolant systems of the ARE are entirely enclosed in a metallic envelope (Fig. 71). This envelope consists of concentric tubing

around all the pipes, and conveniently shaped containers around the other items of equipment, such as valves and pumps. Helium is circulated in the space between the metallic envelope and the main systems to serve two purposes. The helium is continuously sampled by instruments which can detect the presence of small quantities of fuel or NaK vapor and thus give warning if a leak develops in the system (hence the name, "monitoring system"). The flowing helium also acts as a distributor of heat in the following manner. Most of the electric heaters of the main system are on the outside of the envelope. Should a heater failure occur, the flowing helium, by transporting heat from the heated to the unheated areas, will tend to make uniform the heat distributed to the systems and, consequently, to reduce the temperature gradients which would otherwise occur.

The locations where the heat distribution function is particularly critical are in the fuel dump-and-fill lines. As may be noted from the position of the valves in Fig. 2, the fuel dump-and-fill lines may stand full of stagnant fuel during the operation of the reactor. Consequently, in the event of a heater failure, it is possible for the fuel carrier to freeze in these lines and make dumping of the reactor impossible. However, a helium bleed line will be connected to the fuel-carrier line to prevent drainage. The temperature patterns that would occur in these fuel-carrier lines and their envelopes if there were a particularly bad heater failure were

TABLE 17. HEAT LOSSES IN THE PITS   

<table><tr><td></td><td>TOTAL HEAT LOSS (Btu/sec)</td><td>NUMBER OF SPACE COOLERS</td><td>HEAT LOSS PER COOLER (Btu/sec)</td><td>AMBIENT TEMPERATURE (°F)</td></tr><tr><td>All pits</td><td>228</td><td>8</td><td>28.4</td><td>118</td></tr><tr><td>Reactor pit</td><td>28.5</td><td>2</td><td>14.3</td><td>111</td></tr><tr><td>Heat exchanger pit</td><td>128.0</td><td>4</td><td>32.0</td><td>120</td></tr><tr><td>Dump tank pit</td><td>71.1</td><td>2</td><td>35.6</td><td>121</td></tr></table>

![](images/7723f332b383422948e0b07ef4ba8cbecc5bf4a658988d36ee83ca281a91d8cc.jpg)  
Fig. 71. Monitoring System Flow Sheet.

![](images/61b35225cc62c5c7d99bf1572c52c453985f8acbc2da2476c86332905df6d4ff.jpg)

![](images/9fbf2d7d5dbb341b7483d7cd231c0fc538da648898e157b65832b53ae8422122.jpg)

![](images/f89965467280afd20a7f252f58a781447277cbb2aafba63564788f399b7906da.jpg)

![](images/3745e644337bae39baadf564c26cb016a2edc3e545f1c5a4e8c40ed8be062877.jpg)

![](images/ad9a5a1e5f0895a422c784539ea43c5edf6bec70705cedc2a16e1d86bc84ce7c.jpg)  
3 WIRE, 9 UNIT DELTA

![](images/8508215bc657553f430679c144d16e45d1a40e6c7592602344b8c8abdd87c347.jpg)  
2 WIRE, 3 UNIT. USE ONLY WHEN NECESSARY FOR 3 UNITS

![](images/92d702be057f8301207513ac3c22ad95b9873ca82c1499741cb72c46017a0ffe.jpg)  
3 WIRE, 6 UNIT USE ONLY WHEN NECESSARY FOR 6 UNITS

![](images/53e2a75b519311169673cc1dbba7ef9abb68fbcc963552016f427382539a76b1.jpg)  
WIRING DIAGRAMS A, B, AND C   
Fig. 72. Electrical Wiring Diagram for Monitoring Annulus Heaters.

investigated for several helium velocities in the monitoring system. For the investigation, it was assumed that the lines were filled with the fuel carrier. Figure 2 shows the pipeline to the fuel dump tank; Fig. 72 shows a wiring diagram of a typical set of electric heaters, which are in the form of half cylinders and surround the lines shown in Fig. 2. It may be noted from Fig. 72 that three sections of electric heater, each consisting of one half cylinder 8 in. long, are connected in series and that the failure of any one heater results in the failure of the other two. The three heaters are separated so that they are not adjacent to one another. For the type of heater failure considered in the following analysis, it is assumed that two sets of heaters fail and that these are exactly opposite each other on the pipe line.

The system, as set up for analysis, is shown in Fig. 73a. For simplicity, the heat produced by the electric heaters is assumed to be generated directly in the wall of the concentric tube which serves as the metallic envelope. The pipe line is divided into seven sections:

1. Sections 1 and 7 are the unaffected areas of the pipe lines upstream and downstream of the area where the heater failures occurred.   
2. Sections 2, 4, and 6 are the areas where the heaters on both sides of the pipe failed.   
3. Sections 3 and 5 are the areas where the heaters (which separate the failed heaters) are still operative.

In the steady-state condition, the fuel pipe temperature will be equal to the temperature of the flowing helium. Therefore only the heat transfer between the helium, the metallic envelope, and the outside environment needs to be considered. The heat balance in a given section of pipe line in the steady-state condition is given by (Fig. 73b)

$$
Q _ {m} + Q _ {g} + Q _ {\text {H e - m}} = Q _ {m} ^ {\prime} + Q _ {m - e} \tag {25}
$$

and

$$
Q _ {\mathrm {H e}} = Q _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {\prime} + Q _ {\mathrm {H e} - m}, \tag {26}
$$

![](images/e7322a72ca8f2091ff891c3df28a3b66346452151f63888d67f4c5816fd5b33e.jpg)

![](images/9faab60fe01291de8de62c3bb66d3145740fbbce8a738a1637a643639dae0414.jpg)  
Fig. 73. Sections Showing Fuel Pipe and Annulus Walls.

where $Q$ and $Q^{\prime}$ are heat transferred in Btu/sec and the subscripts

$m =$ along the metal of the envelope,

He = in the flowing helium,

He-m = from the helium to the metal of the envelope,

$m - e =$ from the metal of the envelope (through the insulation) to the environment,

$g =$ internally generated.

The various terms in Eqs. 25 and 26 are expressed quite readily as follows: From the common equation for one-dimensional heat conduction,

$$
Q _ {m} - Q _ {m} ^ {\prime} = k _ {m} a _ {m} \frac {\cdot d ^ {2} \theta_ {m}}{d x ^ {2}} d x, \tag {27}
$$

where

$\theta_{m} =$ difference between metal temperature and ambient temperature, F,   
$k =$ thermal conductivity, Btu/sec·ft² (°F/ft),   
$a =$ cross-sectional area, ft²,   
$x =$ distance from origin, ft (the left end of each section, except section 1, is considered as the origin of that particular section; the right end of section 1 is used as the origin of that section).

The heat gained by the helium

$$
Q _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {\prime} - Q _ {\mathrm {H e}} = W _ {\mathrm {H e}} c _ {p _ {\mathrm {H e}}} d \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}}, \tag {28}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \theta_ {\text {H e}} = \text {d i f f e r e n c e b e t w e e n h e l i u m} \\ \text {t e m p e r a t u r e a n d a m b i e n t t e m p e r -} \\ \text {a t u r e ,} ^ {\circ} F, \end{array}
$$

$$
W = \text {w e i g h t f l o w}, \quad \mathrm {l b} / \sec ,
$$

$$
c _ {p} = \text {s p e c i f i c h e a t}, \mathrm {B t u / l b} ^ {\cdot^ {\circ}} \mathrm {F}.
$$

The heat transferred from the helium to the metal

$$
Q _ {\mathrm {H e} - m} = h _ {\mathrm {H e} - m} C _ {m} d x \left(\theta_ {\mathrm {H e}} - \theta_ {m}\right), \tag {29}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} h = \text {h e a t t r a n s f e r c o e f f i c i e n t}, \\ \text {B t u / s e c} \cdot \mathrm {f t} ^ {2 \cdot^ {\circ}} F, \end{array}
$$

$$
C = \text {c i r c u m f e r e n c e o f i n s i d e w a l l o f}
$$

The heat transferred from the metal through the insulation to the environment is a complicated function which was evaluated for various metal temperatures in the section on "Heat Loss Through Insulation," Eqs. 2, 3, and 4, and is shown in Fig. 74. The curve in Fig. 74 can be approximated from about 500 to $1500^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ by a straight line for which the formula is

$$
Q _ {m - e} = b \theta_ {m} + d, \tag {30}
$$

where $b$ and $d$ are constants,

The internal heat generation in the metal is equal to the heat supplied by the electric heaters in those sections in which the heaters are still operative, and it is equal to zero in those

![](images/8fd67cd1d0bc6e1c6daaa57b5d4156ac3b1dd27c3288d46b6b83a6bb649cb85d.jpg)  
Fig. 74. Heat Loss from Insulated 2-in. Pipe vs. Wall Temperature of the Pipe (Sink Temperature, $130^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ ).

sections in which the heaters have failed. The heat supplied by the heaters which are still operative can be varied by the operator. At design conditions, the heaters will be operating at about 5/9 capacity; if thermocouple readings indicate that there were heater failures in the line, the adjacent heaters which were still operative could be run at full power. For the following analysis, however, the more dangerous situation will be considered, namely, that the heater failures go unnoticed and the heaters which are still operative remain at their normal power:

$$
Q _ {g} = q ^ {\prime} a _ {m} d x = P d x, \tag {31}
$$

for sections 1, 3, 5, and 7, and

$$
Q _ {\textbf {g}} = q ^ {\prime} a _ {m} d x = 0,
$$

for sections 2, 4, and 6, where

$$
\begin{array}{c} q ^ {\prime} = \text {u n i t i n t e r n a l h e a t g e n e r a t i o n}, \\ \text {B t u / s e c} \cdot \text {f t} ^ {3}, \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} P = \text {n o r m a l p o w e r i n p u t t o t h e} \\ \text {e l e c t r i c h e a t e r s p e r l i n e a l f o o t}, \\ \text {B t u / s e c} \cdot \text {l i n e a l f t}. \end{array}
$$

Substitution of Eqs. 27 through 31 in Eqs. 25 and 26 gives

$$
\begin{array}{l} k _ {m} a _ {m} \frac {d ^ {2} \theta_ {m}}{d x ^ {2}} + q ^ {\prime} a _ {m} \tag {32} \\ + h _ {\mathrm {H e} - m} C _ {m} \left(\theta_ {\mathrm {H e}} - \theta_ {m}\right) = b \theta_ {m} - d \\ \end{array}
$$

and

$$
\begin{array}{l} W _ {H e} c _ {p _ {H e}} \frac {d \theta_ {H e}}{d x} \tag {33} \\ = - h _ {\text {H e} - m} C _ {m} \left(\theta_ {\text {H e}} - \theta_ {m}\right). \\ \end{array}
$$

Solving Eq. 33 for $\theta_{m}$ and differentiating twice gives

$$
\theta_ {m} = \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}} + \frac {W _ {\mathrm {H e}} c }{h _ {\mathrm {H e} - m} C _ {m}} \frac {d \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}}}{d x} \tag {34}
$$

and

$$
\frac {d ^ {2} \theta_ {m}}{d x ^ {2}} = \frac {d ^ {2} \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}}}{d x ^ {2}} + \frac {W _ {\mathrm {H e} ^ {c} p _ {\mathrm {H e}}}}{h _ {\mathrm {H e} - m} C _ {m}} \frac {d ^ {3} \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}}}{d x ^ {3}}. \tag {35}
$$

Substitution of Eqs. 34 and 35 in Eq. 32 gives

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {d ^ {3} \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}}}{d x ^ {3}} + \frac {h _ {\mathrm {H e} - m} C _ {m}}{W _ {\mathrm {H e}} c _ {p _ {\mathrm {H e}}}} \frac {d ^ {2} \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}}}{d x ^ {2}} \tag {36} \\ - \frac {h _ {\mathrm {H e} - m} C _ {m} + b}{k _ {m} a _ {m}} \frac {d \theta}{d x} - \frac {h _ {\mathrm {H e} - m} C _ {m} b}{k _ {m} a _ {m} W _ {\mathrm {H e}} c _ {p _ {\mathrm {H e}}}} \theta \\ = \frac {h _ {\mathrm {H e} - m} C _ {m}}{k _ {m} W _ {\mathrm {H e}} c _ {p _ {\mathrm {H e}}}} \left(\frac {d}{a _ {m}} - q ^ {\prime}\right), \\ \end{array}
$$

which may be solved quite readily. It is first necessary to evaluate the constant in Eq. 36 for the conditions of the problem. Three different helium velocities were considered: 1, 3, and 10 ft/sec. For these three different velocities, the values of the constants of Eq. 36 are tabulated in the following:

<table><tr><td>Helium velocity, ft/sec</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>10</td></tr><tr><td>hHe-m, Btu/sec·ft2·°F</td><td>3.03 × 10-4</td><td>4.97 × 10-4</td><td>8.54 × 10-4</td></tr><tr><td>Cm, ft</td><td></td><td>0.899</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>WHe, lb/sec</td><td>1.03 × 10-4</td><td>3.09 × 10-4</td><td>10.30 × 10-4</td></tr><tr><td>km, Btu/sec·ft2 (°F/ft)</td><td></td><td>0.00333</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>am, ft2</td><td></td><td>0.00487</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>b</td><td></td><td>2.29 × 10-4</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>d</td><td></td><td>-0.0237</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>q&#x27;, Btu/sec·ft3</td><td></td><td>45.5</td><td>(sections 1, 3, 5, and 7)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>0</td><td>(sections 2, 4, and 6)</td></tr><tr><td>hHe-mCm/WHeCPHe</td><td>2.13</td><td>1.17</td><td>0.601</td></tr><tr><td>hHe-mCm + b/kam</td><td>30.9</td><td>41.6</td><td>61.5</td></tr><tr><td>hHe-mCm b/kam WHeCPHe</td><td>30.1</td><td>16.5</td><td>8.48</td></tr><tr><td>hHe-mCm/km WHeCPHe(d/a_m - q&#x27;)</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>For sections 1, 3, 5, and 7</td><td>-32,300</td><td>-17,600</td><td>-9090</td></tr><tr><td>For sections 2, 4, and 6</td><td>-3120</td><td>-1700</td><td>-879</td></tr></table>

The helium heat transfer coefficient, $h_{\text{He-m}}$ , may be evaluated from the following formula: (14)

$$
h _ {\mathrm {H e} - \mathrm {m}} = 1. 0 2 \frac {k _ {\mathrm {H e}}}{D _ {\mathrm {H e}}} \operatorname {R e} _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {0. 4 5} \Pr_ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {0. 5} \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {0. 1 4} \left(\frac {D _ {\mathrm {H e}}}{L _ {\mathrm {H e}}}\right) _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {0. 4} \left(\frac {D _ {o}}{D _ {i}}\right) ^ {0. 8} \operatorname {G r} _ {\mathrm {H e}} ^ {0. 0 5}, \tag {37}
$$

where

$D =$ equivalent diameter, ft,

$$
\begin{array}{l} D _ {o} = \text {o u t s i d e d i a m e t e r o f h e l i u m} \\ \text {a n n u l u s , f t ,} \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} D _ {i} = \text {i n s i d e d i a m e t e r o f h e l i u m} \\ \text {a n n u l u s , f t ,} \end{array}
$$

$$
\mathrm {R e} = \text {R e y n o l d s} ^ {\prime} \text {n u m b e r},
$$

$$
\Pr = \text {P r a n d t l} ^ {\prime} \text {s} \text {n u m b e r},
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}} = \text {r a t i o o f v i s c o s i t y a t b u l k} \\ \text {t e m p e r a t u r e t o v i s c o s i t y a t s u r - f a c e t e m p e r a t u r e ,} \end{array}
$$

$$
L = \text {l e n g t h o f p a s s a g e , f t},
$$

$$
\mathrm {G r} = \text {G r a s h o f} ^ {\prime} \text {s n u m b e r}.
$$

Having evaluated the constants in Eq. 36, the solution of the equation is straightforward. Once an expression for $\theta_{\mathrm{He}}$ is found, $\theta_{m}$ can be evaluated from Eq. 34. The solutions for helium velocities of 1, 3, and 10 ft/sec follow:

1. For a helium velocity of 1 ft/sec,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}} = n _ {1} e ^ {5. 1 0 x} + n _ {2} e ^ {- 6. 2 9 x} \tag {38} \\ + n _ {3} e ^ {- 0. 9 4 1 x} + K \\ \end{array}
$$

and

$$
\begin{array}{l} \theta_ {m} = 3. 3 9 n _ {1} e ^ {5. 1 0 x} - 1. 9 5 n _ {2} e ^ {- 6. 2 9 x} \tag {39} \\ + 0. 5 5 9 e ^ {- 0. 9 4 1 x} + K, \\ \end{array}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} n = \text {a r b i t r a r y c o n s t a n t}, \\ K = 1 0 7 0 \text {f o r s e c t i o n s} 1, 3, 5, \text {a n d} 7 \\ = 1 0 4 \text {f o r s e c t i o n s} 2, 4, \text {a n d} 6. \\ \end{array}
$$

2. For a helium velocity of 3 ft/sec,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathcal {E} _ {\mathrm {H e}} = n _ {4} e ^ {6. 1 0 x} + n _ {5} e ^ {- 6. 8 7 x} \tag {40} \\ + n _ {6} e ^ {- 0. 3 9 2 x} + K \\ \end{array}
$$

and

$$
\begin{array}{l} \text {E} _ {m} = 6. 2 3 n _ {4} e ^ {6. 1 0 x} - 4. 9 0 n _ {5} e ^ {- 6. 8 7 x} \tag {41} \\ + 0. 6 6 4 n _ {6} e ^ {- 0 \cdot 3 9 2 x} + K. \\ \end{array}
$$

3. For a helium velocity of 10 ft/sec,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}} = n _ {7} e ^ {7. 6 2 x} + n _ {8} e ^ {- 8. 0 8 x} \tag {42} \\ + n _ {9} e ^ {- 0. 1 3 7 x} + K \\ \end{array}
$$

and

$$
\begin{array}{l} \theta_ {m} = 1 3. 7 n _ {7} e ^ {7. 6 2 x} - 1 2. 4 n _ {8} e ^ {- 8. 0 8 x} \tag {43} \\ + 0. 7 7 2 n _ {9} e ^ {- 0. 1 3 7 x} + K. \\ \end{array}
$$

There are nine arbitrary constants in Eqs. 38 through 43. These constants have a different value in each of the

seven sections; there are therefore, altogether, 63 arbitrary constants. These may be evaluated by means of the following boundary conditions, which apply to all three helium velocities:

$$
\lim  _ {x _ {1} \rightarrow - \infty} \mathcal {O} _ {\text {H e}} \tag {44}
$$

is bounded. Since each section has a different origin (see definition of $x$ , following Eq. 27), the subscript on $x$ refers to the number of the section being considered. Thus

$$
\begin{array}{l} \left. \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}} \right| _ {x _ {1} = 0} = \left. \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}} \right| _ {x _ {2} = 0}, (45) \\ \left. \theta_ {m} \right| _ {\textit {x} _ {1} = 0} = \left. \theta_ {m} \right| _ {\textit {x} _ {2} = 0}, (46) \\ \left. \frac {d \theta_ {m}}{d x} \right| _ {x _ {1} = 0} = \left. \frac {d \theta_ {m}}{d x} \right| _ {x _ {2} = 0}, (47) \\ \left. \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}} \right| _ {x _ {r} = 2 / 3} = \left. \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}} \right| _ {x _ {r + 1} = 0}, (48) \\ r = 2, 3, 4, 5, 6 \\ \left. \theta_ {m} \right| _ {\mathbf {x} _ {r} = 2 / 3} = \left. \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}} \right| _ {\mathbf {x} _ {r + 1} = 0}, (49) \\ r = 2, 3, 4, 5, 6 \\ \left. \frac {d \theta_ {m}}{d x} \right| _ {x _ {r} = 2 / 3} = \left. \frac {d \theta_ {m}}{d x} \right| _ {x _ {r + 1} = 0}, (50) \\ r = 2, 3, 4, 5, 6 \\ \lim  _ {x _ {7} \rightarrow \infty} \theta_ {\mathrm {H e}} (51) \\ \end{array}
$$

is bounded.

Equations 44 through 51 were used to evaluate the nine arbitrary constants, $n_1$ to $n_9$ , for each of the seven sections. The results of the evaluation are given in Table 18.

The values of the arbitrary constants and Eqs. 38 through 43 were used to calculate the temperatures of the helium and the metallic envelope for all positions along the pipe line for the three helium velocities. The results are shown in Figs. 75, 76, and 77. The minimum helium temperature

# Chapter 7

# HELIUM SUPPLY AND VENTING SYSTEM

# TRUE HOLDUP OF FISSION GASES IN TANKS

The fission gases removed along with helium through the various gas vents in the fuel circuit are held up in the pipes and tanks of the off-gas system (Fig. 79) until the activity in the fission gases is reduced sufficiently to permit their being discharged into the stack. The holdup time of the gases in pipe lines or similar shapes of large length-to-diameter ratio is readily calculated as the pipe volume divided by the gas volume flow rate. All the gas is held up substantially the same amount of time. In large tanks or similar shapes of small length-to-diameter ratio, the average gas holdup time is the same as in a pipe line of similar volume. However, because of the mixing of the gas in the tank, not all the gas is held up for substantially equal periods; that is, some of the gas leaving the holdup tank is held up for much less than average time and some for much more than average. The true activity of the gases leaving the holdup tank (Fig. 80) of the off-gas system has been calculated and compared with the activity which would be present if all gases were held up for equal periods.

The mixing of the gas in the tank is due to convective and diffusive forces and occurs fairly rapidly compared with the average holdup time in the tank. Thereasonably conservative assumption was made that the gas entering the tank mixes instantaneously with the gas in the tank. The following definitions were made for the calculations:

$$
\begin{array}{l} N = \text {f l o w r a t e o f g a s}, \text {c f m}, \\ V = \text {t a n k v o l u m e , f t} ^ {3}, \\ \begin{array}{l} C (t) = \text {c o n c e n t r a t i o n o f g a s e s o f} \\ \text {a g e t , t h a t i s , C (t)} d t \text {e q u a l s} \\ \text {t h e r a t i o o f t h e v o l u m e o f g a s} \\ \text {w i t h a g e f r o m t t o t + d t} \\ \text {c o m p a r e d w i t h t h e t o t a l t a n k} \\ \text {v o l u m e , V (a g e i s u s e d h e r e t o} \end{array} \\ \end{array}
$$

mean time measured from the time the gases enter the off-gas system),

$$
t = \text {t i m e}, \min .
$$

Then,

(1) $C(t + dt)d\cdot t$

$$
= C (t) d t - C (t) d t \frac {N}{V} d t,
$$

(2) $\frac{C(t + dt) - C(t)}{dt} = -C(t)\frac{N}{V},$   
(3) $\frac{dC(t)}{dt} = -C(t)\frac{N}{V},$   
(4) $C(t) = K e^{*(N / V)t}$ ,

where $K$ is an arbitrary constant.

When $t = t_0$ , where $t_0$ is the age of the youngest gases in the tank,

(5) $C(t)dt = \frac{N}{V} dt$

and

(6) $C(t_0) = \frac{N}{V}$ .

Substituting Eq. 6 in Eq. 4 and solving for $K$ gives

(7) $\frac{N}{V} = K e^{-(N / V)t_0}$ ,   
(8) $K = \frac{N}{V} e^{(N / V)t_0},$   
(9) $C(t) = \frac{N}{V} e^{-(N / V)(t - t_0)}$ .

The energy, $E(t)$ , in the fission gases xenon and krypton (bromine and iodine removed previously) at time $t$ is plotted against $t$ in Figs. 81, 82, and 83 for the separate xenon and krypton isotopes and for the total of these isotopes. For the determination of $E(t)$ , it was considered that there had been 200 hr of reactor operation prior to time zero and that the fission gases had subsequently decayed time $t$ .

MATERIAL AND MATERIAL SPECIFICATIONS

UNCLASSIFIED

DWG-E-A-3-8-52A

![](images/6da58742df44ab1d98a83c89b11c33afc82fa0fe83c3fbeec448c127dba72864.jpg)  
Fig. 79. Off-Gas Disposal System.

![](images/de506bc7efc517608e28fc486709924300f5728d96e57450b19a589122b7b07f.jpg)

![](images/1e18cc4f0dcef8f2568d705395dd7e9cafa3162b3d7bf6065ae8e777f1b352db.jpg)  
Fig. 80. Off-Gas Disposal System Drum.

If $R$ is the ratio of the true activity of the gases leaving the holdup tank to the activity which would be present if all the gases were held up equally,

$$
R = \frac {\int_ {t _ {0}} ^ {t _ {p}} \frac {N}{V} e ^ {- (N / V) (t - t _ {0})} E (t) d t}{E \left(t _ {0} + \frac {V}{N}\right)}, \tag {10}
$$

where $t_p$ is the age of the oldest gases in the tank. The total energy in all the fission gases developed may be used since the final answer desired is a ratio.

It is important to note that for Eq. 10 it is assumed that all the

gases in the tank contain equal amounts of fission gases. This is obviously not true of the gas originally in the tank. Thus, at the beginning of operation, the value of $R$ is actually less than is predicted by Eq. 10. The highest possible values of $R$ will occur after long periods of operation, and Eq. 10 will therefore be evaluated with $t_p$ sufficiently large for the contribution to the total activity of the gases of age greater than $t_p$ to be neglected.

The curve of $E(t)$ vs. $t$ shown in Fig. 83 for the total energy of all xenon and krypton isotopes was broken up into a series of $M$ segments between $t_0$ and $t_p$ , and an equation of exponential form was fitted to each segment.

![](images/b6c95a83e51b3c6b3333bb13e6c5f1d6b3ade59b58b5784a529b5c48bd569f1f.jpg)  
Fig. 81. Integrated Xenon Activity, $F(t)$ , vs. Time after Reactor Shutdown.

Thus, for the nth segment,

$$
E _ {n} (t) = A _ {n} e ^ {- b _ {n} t}, \quad t _ {n - 1} \leqslant t <   t _ {n}, \tag {11}
$$

and then

$$
R = \frac {\sum_ {n = 1} ^ {M} \int_ {t _ {n - 1}} ^ {t _ {n}} \frac {N}{V} e ^ {- (N / V) (t - t _ {0})} A _ {n} e ^ {- b _ {n} t} d t}{E \left(t _ {0} + \frac {V}{N}\right)}, \tag {12}
$$

where $t_{M} = t_{p}$

$$
R = \frac {1}{E \left(t _ {0} + \frac {V}{N}\right)} \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {M} A _ {n} \frac {N}{V} \int_ {t _ {n - 1}} ^ {t _ {n}} e ^ {- [ (N / V) + b _ {n} ] t + (N / V) t _ {0}} d t, \tag {13}
$$

$$
R = \frac {1}{E \left(t _ {0} + \frac {V}{N}\right)} \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {M} - \frac {A _ {n} \frac {N}{V}}{\frac {N}{V} + b _ {n}} \left[ e ^ {- \left[ (N / V) + b _ {n} \right] t + (N / V) t _ {0}} \right] _ {t _ {n - 1}} ^ {t _ {n}}, \tag {14}
$$

$$
R = \frac {\frac {N}{V} e ^ {(N / V) t _ {0}}}{E \left(t _ {0} + \frac {V}{N}\right)} \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {M} - \frac {A _ {n}}{\frac {N}{V} + b _ {n}} \left[ e ^ {*} \left[ (N / V) + b _ {n} \right] t _ {n} - e ^ {*} \left[ (N / V) + b _ {n} \right] t _ {n - 1} \right].
$$

![](images/57d1c681012c0773e94ec4b54e4580b28eaab3bd79cfed2e4b8ef53d9fb64ff9.jpg)  
Fig. 82. Integrated Krypton Activity, $F(t)$ , vs. Time after Reactor Shutdown.

The data and results of the calculations for the particular problem of the first holdup tank in the off-gas system are given in Table 19.

As a check on whether the intervals selected were small enough, the interval between $t_0$ and $t_1$ was divided into two parts. Recalculation of the data for the smaller intervals is given in Table 20.

The value of 295 checks very closely with the value of 296 calculated for the original intervals, and thus the intervals selected were probably small enough.

The final answer, then, is that the actual activity of the gases coming out of the tank is $23\%$ greater than the activity which would be present

if all the gases were held up for equal periods.

# TEMPERATURES IN THE HELIUM VENT LINES CONTAINING FISSION GASES

The off-gas system carries gases from the surge tanks, the dump tank, and the pits to the exhaust stack (Fig. 79). At times, a portion of these gases will be radioactive fission products. The gas flow rate is generally quite low, and the question arises as to what temperatures will exist on the off-gas system with essentially stagnant radioactive gases in the system. The total energy of all the fission gases produced after 200 hr of reactor operation at 3 megawatts is about 14.8 Btu/sec for a delay time of 1 l/2 minutes.(22) The

Data:

$$

$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} N = \text {g a s f l o w} \\ = 1 / 2 \mathrm {c f h} = 1 / 1 2 0 \mathrm {c f m} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} V = \text {t a n k v o l u m e} \\ = 7. 5 \mathrm {f t} ^ {3} \\ \end{array}

energy is divided among the fission gases as follows:

This energy may be distributed over the gas volume in the surge tanks, which is about 1.3 ft³, or it may come out of the fuel in the dump tank, in which case it will be distributed over a greater gas diluent volume. The

maximum energy release in the off-gas system, which occurs in the release of gases from the surge tanks, is therefore about 11.4 Btu/sec·ft<sup>3</sup>.

The fission gases on leaving the surge tanks enter a fuel vapor trap (Fig. 84) and then a 2-in. IPS pipe. The trap and the pipe are the critical points in the off-gas system, since they have the greatest volume-to-surface ratio and since the fission gases are youngest at these points. The energy dissipated per square foot of outside surface by radiation and

$$
\begin{array}{l} Q = \frac {0 . 1 7 3}{3 6 0 0} \in \left[ \left(\frac {T _ {s}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} - \left(\frac {T _ {e}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} \right] \tag {15} \\ + h _ {e} \left(T _ {s} - T _ {e}\right), \\ \end{array}

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} Q = \text {h e a t} \quad \text {d i s s i p a t e d}, \quad \mathrm {B t u / s e c} \cdot \mathrm {f t} ^ {2}, \\ \varepsilon = \text {e m i s s i v i t y}, \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} T _ {s} = \text {s u r f a c e t e m p e r a t u r e}, ^ {\circ} R, \\ T _ {e} = \text {e n v i r o n m e n t t e m p e r a t u r e}, ^ {\circ} R, \\ h _ {c} = \begin{array}{l} \text {f r e e c o n v e c t i o n h e a t t r a n s f e r} \\ \text {c o e f f i c i e n t , B t u / s e c} \cdot \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} \cdot {} ^ {\circ} F. \end{array} \\ \end{array}
$$

![](images/e4b8eea8951f392928c5921f70deb33ddb181dc999846ac105dff80cb4d4f61d.jpg)  
Fig. 84. Surge Tank Vapor Trap.

The free convection heat transfer coefficient is determined from the data of Nusselt, $^{(18)}$ McAdams, and W.J. King, $^{(19)}$ who correlated Nusselt's number with Grashof's and Prandtl's numbers (as given by Jakob $^{(14)}$ ).

The pertinent physical dimensions and unit energies of the fuel vapor traps and the 2-in. IPS pipe are listed in the following:

<table><tr><td></td><td>FUEL VAPOR TRAP (per trap)</td><td>2-in. IPS PIPE</td></tr><tr><td>Gas volume, ft3</td><td>0.13</td><td>0.0233 (per ft)</td></tr><tr><td>Surface, ft2</td><td>2.6</td><td>0.622 (per ft)</td></tr><tr><td>Equivalent outside diameter, ft</td><td>0.55</td><td>0.198</td></tr><tr><td>Total energy, Btu/sec</td><td>1.48</td><td>0.265 (per ft)</td></tr><tr><td>Energy per ft2of surface, Btu/sec·ft2</td><td>0.246</td><td>0.427</td></tr></table>

For proper operation, the fuel vapor traps must be maintained at $1500^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . The traps are therefore covered with electric heaters and insulated. The heater power is controlled by thermocouples which measure the trap temperature. The heat input required to hold a trap at $1500^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ is about 0.71 Btu/sec (cf., chap. 6, section on "Heat Loss Through Insulation"). The energy loss through the insulation would more than be balanced by the energy in the fission gases, and the heaters could

be shut off as the fuel vapor trap began to heat up slowly. The heat capacity of the vapor trap, including contained liquid, is about 13.2 Btu/°F. A tabulation of the energy dissipations and of the temperatures of the vapor trap at various times is given in Table 21. The vapor trap temperature would be increased by only about $40^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ at the end of 20 min, and the heaters would have to be turned on again as the fission gases further decayed.

The 2-in. IPS pipe is neither heated nor insulated, and therefore Eq. 15 may be used to calculate the pipe outside temperature (the inside temperature is not greatly different). For an emissivity of 0.5, the pipe outside temperature necessary to dissipate 0.265 Btu/sec·linealft is about $380^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

# VACUUM PUMP PERFORMANCE

There are two vacuum pumps in the off-gas system (Figs. 79 and 85). They serve many purposes, but their most severe requirement is that they must reduce the pressure in the fuel system sufficiently to permit the boiling off of NaK which cannot be completely drained from this system (NaK is circulated in the fuel system for precleaning and hot testing). Figures 86 and 87 give the experimentally determined performance of the two vacuum pumps. Figure 86 shows the variation of pressure with time in a pump-down test of a $12.3\text{-ft}^3$ tank; Fig. 87 shows the rate of pressure rise due to leakage after the

Fig. 86. Vacuum Pump Performance.

pumps are shut off. From these curves, curves of the variation of rate of change of pressure with pressure were drawn, as shown in Fig. 88.

If a leak develops in the system, it may be desirable to remove all the NaK from the system. In order to boil off NaK at $1200^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , a pressure of about $100 \mathrm{~mmHg}$ or less must be maintained. The following calculations were made to determine the maximum

size of leak which might exist and still permit the vacuum pumps to maintain a pressure of 100 mm Hg.

The process of evacuating gas from a tank or containing system is isothermal, and therefore

$$
P V = \text {c o n s t a n t} = K \tag {16}
$$

or

$$
P = \frac {K}{V}, \tag {17}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} P = \text {p r e s s u r e}, \mathrm {m m} \mathrm {H g}, \\ V = \text {v o l u m e}, \quad \mathrm {f t} ^ {3}, \\ K = \text {a c o n s t a n t}. \\ \end{array}
$$

![](images/ce51c22798c8c95755c7d8ed4a97ddc86d22d64b55b918c0a57633091dabfbb2.jpg)  
Fig. 87. Leakage Test.

Differentiating with respect to time, t, gives

$$
\frac {d P}{d t} = - \frac {K}{V ^ {2}} \frac {d V}{d t}, \tag {18}
$$

and dividing both sides by $P$ gives

$$
\frac {\frac {d P}{d t}}{P} = - \frac {K}{P V ^ {2}} \frac {d V}{d t}. \tag {19}
$$

However, $K = PV$ , and therefore

$$
\frac {\frac {d P}{d t}}{P} = - \frac {\frac {d V}{d t}}{V}. \tag {20}
$$

Since the volume of the tank tested was 12.3 ft³, Eq. 20 becomes

![](images/711ade63f67b9faa94de7cb664c25c65976bf1e6edb4de3bcb35fe748c42b747.jpg)  
Fig. 88. Vacuum Pump Performance. Volume of test system, 12.3 ft<sup>3</sup>.

$$
\left. \frac {\frac {d P}{d t}}{P} \frac {\frac {d V}{d t}}{1 2 . 3} \text {o r} \left| \frac {d V}{d t} \right| = \frac {1 2 . 3}{P} \right| \frac {d P}{d t}. \tag {21}
$$

The values of volumetric flow, $dV/dt$ , as calculated from the pump-down test results, are given in Table 22. The values are for pump inlet conditions; that is, the pressure is equal to the system pressure and the temperature is approximately $560^{\circ}\mathrm{R}$ , since the gas is cooled before it reaches the vacuum pumps.

The vacuum pumps are of the rotary positive displacement type, and it would therefore be expected that the volumetric flow would decrease slightly with decreasing pressure, since the leakage past the rotor increases. It is apparent from the calculated values of volumetric flow, $dV/dt$ , that the curves of Fig. 88 are probably somewhat inaccurate. The inaccuracy is not entirely unexpected because the information for Fig. 88 was obtained by measuring the slopes of the curves of Fig. 86, and the measurements were not highly accurate. The accuracy is of the order of $\pm 10\%$ , however, which is sufficient for the purpose of the problem.

The values in Table 22 for volumetric flow are for pumping air. The leakage past the rotor when pumping helium may be somewhat higher than when pumping air, but it is probably accurate enough to assume that the volumetric flows are the same for both helium and air.

The volumetric flow of helium through a leak can be approximated by considering a hypothetical "leak" in

the form of a round orifice:

$$
\left(\frac {d V}{d t}\right) _ {\text {o r i f i c e}} = C A v, \tag {22}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \left(\frac {d V}{d t}\right) _ {\text {o r i f i c e}} = \begin{array}{l} \text {o r i f i c e v o l u m e t r i c f l o w ,} \\ \text {c f s ,} \end{array} \\ C = \text {o r i f i c e} = 0. 6 0, \\ A = \text {o r i f i c e} \quad \text {a r e a}, \quad \mathrm {f t} ^ {2}, \\ \begin{array}{l} v = \text {g a s v e l o c i t y i n o r i f i c e ,} \\ \text {f t / s e c .} \end{array} \\ \end{array}
$$

The critical pressure ratio for a perfect gas is given by

$$
r _ {C} = \left(\frac {2}{\gamma + 1}\right) ^ {\gamma / (\gamma - 1)}; \tag {23}
$$

thus with $\gamma_{\mathrm{He}} =$ ratio of specific heats $= 1.67$

$$
r _ {C} = 0. 4 8 7.
$$

The throat pressure for sonic velocity is therefore

$$
\rho_ {t h} = 0. 4 8 7 \times 7 6 0 = 3 7 0 \mathrm {m m H g}. \tag {24}
$$

The throat temperature is

$$
T _ {t h} = T _ {\text {a m b}} \left(r _ {C}\right) ^ {(\gamma - 1) / \gamma}, \tag {25}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} T _ {\text {a m b}} = \text {a m b i e n t t e m p e r a t u r e i n t h e} \\ \text {h e l i u m m o n i t o r i n g a n n u l u s} = \\ 1 6 6 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {R}, \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{r l} T _ {t h} & = \text {t h r o a t t e m p e r a t u r e} = 1 6 6 0 \\ (0. 4 8 7) ^ {(\gamma - 1) / \gamma} & = 1 2 3 5 ^ {\circ} R. \end{array}
$$

For system pressures less than $370 \, \text{mmHg}$ , the volumetric flow through the orifice will be constant. The velocity in the throat will always be

TABLE 22. CALCULATED RESULTS OF THE PUMP-DOWN TEST OF A 12.3-ft ${}^{3}$ TANK   

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">P, SYSTEM (mm Hg)</td><td colspan="2">dP/dt (mm Hg/sec)</td><td colspan="3">dV/dt (cfs)</td></tr><tr><td>Pump A</td><td>Pump B</td><td>Pump A</td><td>Pump B</td><td>Pumps A + B</td></tr><tr><td>300</td><td>5.95</td><td>5.55</td><td>0.244</td><td>0.277</td><td>0.571</td></tr><tr><td>150</td><td>2.56</td><td>2.28</td><td>0.210</td><td>0.187</td><td>0.397</td></tr><tr><td>100</td><td>1.63</td><td>1.41</td><td>0.200</td><td>0.173</td><td>0.373</td></tr><tr><td>50</td><td>0.86</td><td>0.70</td><td>0.211</td><td>0.172</td><td>0.383</td></tr></table>

sonic velocity, which is given by

$$
v = \sqrt {g \gamma R T}, \tag {26}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} g = 3 2. 2 f t / \sec^ {2}, \\ \gamma = 1. 6 7, \\ R = \text {g a s} = 3 8 6, \\ T = \text {t e m p e r a t u r e} = 1 2 3 5 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {R}, \\ \end{array}
$$

and thus

$$
v = 5 0 6 0 f t / s e c.
$$

Therefore

(27) $\left(\frac{dV}{dt}\right)_{\text{orific}} = 0.60A \times 5060 = 3040A.$

# Chapter 8

# DUMP AND FILL SYSTEM

# FUEL DUMP TANK COOLING

The heat generated in the fuel after shutdown of the reactor can easily be taken up by the heat capacity of the fuel and the fuel system without overheating. However, once the fuel is in the hot fuel dump tank, it is desirable to keep the temperature of this tank below a reasonable level. Therefore heat is removed by allowing helium to pass through the 91 coolant tubes of the tank (Fig. 89).

It is postulated that the maximum heat removal rate necessary is 50 Btu/sec. The problem of cooling this tank does not lend itself readily to analytical calculation. However, the cooling was analyzed by free-convection calculations and by chimney-effect methods. Both methods are outlined below.

The equation for free convection heat transfer is

(1) $Q_{c} = hA\theta$

where

$Q_{c} =$ heat removed by convection, Btu/sec,

$h =$ free-convection heat transfer coefficient taken at mean helium temperature, Btu/sec·ft².°F,

$A = 91$ times surface area of inside of a coolant tube, ft²,

$\theta =$ temperature difference between the inside surface of tube and mean helium temperature, $^\circ \mathrm{F}$

The value of the free-convection heat transfer coefficient for each helium temperature was calculated from the Nusselt's number. The Nusselt's number is given as a function of the product of Grashof's and Prandtl's numbers on p. 525 of ref. 14. It is realized that the length-to-diameter ratio of the coolant tube is too great for use of these correlations; however, they were the only correlations available, and an order of magnitude estimate could be gained by using them.

The inside diameter of each coolant tube is 2.067 in. and the length is 3.1 feet. The temperature of the inside surface was taken to be $1325^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ over the entire length of the tube.

Mean temperature values were assumed for the helium in the coolant tube, and from these and Eq. 1, a curve was obtained of heat removed vs. mean helium temperature. The mean temperature corresponding to a heat removal rate of 50 Btu/sec is $1025^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . This corresponds to an outlet temperature of $1920^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , since the inlet temperature was assumed to be $135^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . A lower outlet temperature would mean a greater heat removal rate. Thus, apparently the dump tank will be adequately cooled.

The chimney-effect method makes use of the fact that a buoyant force acts on the air in the coolant tube because of a density difference. This buoyant force is

(2) $F = V(\rho_{\text{amb}} - \rho_{\text{m}})$ ,

where

$$
F = \text {b u o y a n t} \quad \text {f o r c e}, \quad \mathrm {l b},
$$

$$
V = \text {v o l u m e}, \quad \mathrm {f t} ^ {3},
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \rho_ {a m b} = d e n s i t y o f h e l i u m a t r o o m \\ \text {t e m p e r a t u r e , l b / f t ^ {3} ,} \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \rho_ {m} = d e n s i t y o f h e l i u m a t m e a n \\ \text {t e m p e r a t u r e o f h e l i u m i n t u b e ,} \\ \text {l b / f t ^ {3}}. \end{array}
$$

From Eq. 2, the pressure acting on the column of helium is

(3) $\triangle P = \frac{F}{A}$ ,

where

$\triangle P =$ difference in upward and downward pressures, $\mathbf{lb} / \mathbf{ft}^2$

$A =$ cross-sectional area of coolant tube, ft².

The pressure can also be expressed by

$$
\Delta P = 4 f \left(\frac {L}{D}\right) \frac {\rho_ {\text {感}} v ^ {2}}{2 g}, \tag {4}
$$

![](images/7fe0141522f3c44cc4c80142232c3c6e2e163019d4f9d61409e78ed9ead68dd3.jpg)

![](images/ff6b9b0329a510aa08ce0d198a99d2304c760a9bfd22bbe8a439e6c54cf4011d.jpg)  
Fig. 89. Fuel Dump Tank.

where

$$
f = \frac {1 6}{N _ {R}}
$$

= friction factor for laminar flow, dimensionless,

$L =$ length of coolant tube, ft,

$D =$ equivalent diameter of coolant tube, ft,

$\pmb{v} =$ helium velocity, ft/sec,

$g =$ gravitational acceleration, 32.2 ft/sec²,

$N_{R} =$ Reynolds' number, dimensionless,

The velocity of helium through the tube is, from Eq. 4,

$$
v = \left(\frac {2 g D}{4 f L \rho_ {\text {题}}}\right) ^ {1 / 2}. \tag {5}
$$

The amount of heat removed per tube can be expressed by

$$
Q = w c _ {p} \Delta T \tag {6}
$$

and

$$
Q = h A _ {s} \theta . \tag {7}
$$

In Eqs. 6 and 7,

$$
w = \nu A \rho_ {\text {最}},
$$

and

$$
h = 1. 8 6 \frac {k}{D} \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) ^ {0. 1 4} \left(\frac {4 w c _ {p}}{\pi k L}\right) ^ {1 / 3},
$$

where

$Q =$ amount of heat removed, Btu/sec,

$w =$ weight flow rate, lb/sec,

$c_{p} =$ specific heat, Btu/1b·°F,

$\Delta T =$ difference between inlet and outlet temperature of helium, ${}^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$

$h =$ heat transfer coefficient, Btu/sec $\cdot \mathrm{ft}^2\cdot \circ \mathrm{F}$

$A_{s} =$ surface area of inside of tube, ft²,

$\theta = \log$ mean temperature difference,

$k =$ thermal conductivity of helium, Btu/sec·ft²·°F,

$\mu =$ viscosity of helium at mean temperature, lb/sec·ft,

$\mu_{s} =$ viscosity of helium at surface temperature of tube, lb/sec·ft.

Equations 6 and 7 were solved by assuming various helium outlet temper

atures, and the values thus obtained for each equation were plotted against helium outlet temperature. From these curves, the helium outlet temperature was found to be $805^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , the heat removal rate per tube is 0.59 Btu/sec, and the total heat removed is $91 \times 0.59 = 53.7$ Btu/sec.

# HEATING OF FILL TANK WITH CENTRALLY LOCATED DIP TUBE

Upon heating a flush-and-fill tank containing helium, the temperature of the dip tube will lag the temperature of the helium and metal walls of the tank during the heating process (Fig. 90). The temperature of the helium will follow very closely the temperature of the tank walls.

Heat is transmitted to the dip tube by free convection of the helium and by thermal radiation from the wall. The difference in temperature between the dip tube and the wall has been determined at various wall temperatures for certain heating rates.

The net heat input to the tank, excluding loss, is

$$
Q = q _ {T} + q _ {H} + q _ {D},
$$

where

$$
q _ {T} = \left(W c _ {p}\right) _ {T} \frac {d t}{d \tau} \cong \left(W c _ {p}\right) _ {T} \frac {\Delta t}{\Delta \tau},
$$

and

$$
q _ {H} = \left(W _ {C _ {p}}\right) _ {H} \frac {d t}{d \tau} \simeq \left(W _ {C _ {p}}\right) _ {H} \frac {\Delta t}{\Delta \tau}.
$$

The term $dt / d\tau$ can be replaced by $\Delta t / \Delta \tau$ without much error if $\Delta \tau$ is small.

The value of $q_{D}$ is given by

$$
\begin{array}{l} q _ {D} = h A _ {D} \theta \\ + \frac {0 . 1 7 3}{3 6 0 0} A _ {D} \in \left[ \left(\frac {T _ {T}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} - \left(\frac {T _ {D}}{1 0 0}\right) ^ {4} \right]. \\ \end{array}
$$

The emissivity is

$$
\epsilon = \frac {1}{\frac {1}{\epsilon_ {D}} + \frac {D _ {D}}{D _ {T}} \left(\frac {1}{\epsilon_ {T} - 1}\right)}.
$$

![](images/7cbdf7a81706b6eb7f70271b513fdcc4b4fc9cf469163a4c590ff1e29816b958.jpg)  
Fig. 90. Fill Tank.

In these equations, the symbols have the following meanings and units:

$Q =$ total net heat input, Btu/sec,

$q =$ heat input of component, Btu/sec,

$W =$ weight of material, lb,

$c_{p} =$ specific heat of material, Btu/1b $^{\circ}$ F,

$\triangle t =$ temperature change, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{F},$

$\Delta \tau =$ time interval, sec,

$h =$ free-convection heat transfer coefficient, Btu/sec·ft²·°F,

$A_{D} =$ outside surface area of dip tube, ft²,

$\theta =$ temperature difference between helium and dip tube, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{F}$

$\epsilon =$ radiation emissivity, dimensionless,

$T =$ absolute temperature, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{R}$

$D =$ diameter, ft,

and the subscripts

$T = \mathrm{tank}$

$H =$ helium,

$D =$ dip tube.

The free-convection heat transfer coefficient can be calculated from the Nusselt's number. A curve of Nusselt's number vs. the product of Grashof's and Prandtl's numbers is given on p. 525 of ref. 14.

The heat input to the tank wall and the helium is

$$
q _ {T} + q _ {H} = Q - q _ {D}.
$$

Therefore

$$
\Delta \tau = \frac {Q - q _ {D}}{\left(W c _ {p}\right) _ {H} \Delta t + \left(W c _ {p}\right) _ {T} \Delta t};
$$

the change in temperature of the dipt tube is

$$
\Delta t _ {D} = \frac {q _ {D} \Delta \tau}{\left(W c _ {p}\right) _ {D}};
$$

and the temperature at any time is

$$
t _ {D} = t _ {1} + \Delta t _ {D}, \tag {19}
$$

where $t_1$ is the temperature at the beginning of the time interval (°F).

The emissivity of the tank wall was taken to be the same as that of the dip tube. The value used in making the calculation was

$$
\epsilon_ {T} = \epsilon_ {D} = 0. 5 0.
$$

The dip tube was taken to be a 2-in., schedule 40 pipe that extended to the bottom of the tank.

In calculating the temperature differences between the wall and the dip tube, net input heat rates of 50, 12, and 8 Btu/sec were used. The results of these calculations are shown in Fig. 91.

![](images/a802557ef60923f822eeda54dc6a241d75780d1fc721711730fe98091ffbb8c2.jpg)  
Fig. 91. Results of Fill Tank Preheat Calculations.

# Chapter 9

# OTHER INVESTIGATIONS

# AFTERHEAT IN FISSION PRODUCTS

In order to analyze many of the problems which arise in the ARE, it is necessary to know the decay energy of the fission products at various times after reactor shutdown. Reference 23 gives the average dis-integration energy of the fission products as a function of time after fission. This curve (ref. 23, Fig. 6a), reproduced as Fig. 92 of this report, cannot be used directly, however, because the fissions in the reactor occur over a considerable period of time and the fission products, consequently, have a wide range of ages. The decay energy of the fission products

may be expressed as

$$
Q = K \int_ {t _ {1}} ^ {t _ {2}} q (t) P d t, \tag {1}
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} Q = \text {e n e r g y i n t h e f i s s i o n p r o d u c t s ,} \\ \text {B t u / s e c ,} \end{array}
$$

$$
q (t) = \text {t h e a v e r a g e d i s i n t e g r a t i o n} \quad \text {e n e r g y o f t h e f i s s i o n p r o d u c t s} \quad \text {a f u n c t i o n o f t i m e a f t e r} \quad \text {f i s s i o n (F i g . 9 2) , M e v / s e c} \cdot \text {f i s s i o n},
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} P = \text {f i s s i o n r a t e i n t h e r e a c t o r}, \\ \text {f i s s i o n / s e c}, \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} K = \text {a c o n s t a n t f o r c o n v e r t i n g} \\ \text {M e v / s e c t o B t u / s e c} = 1. 5 1 7 \\ \times 1 0 ^ {* 1 6}, \end{array}
$$

![](images/9c83a4940a96b422b664fef473bb8b1176e2a71366acf7d91a5d5096e68c69a9.jpg)  
Fig. 92. Disintegration Energy per Fission vs. Time after Shutdown.

$t =$ age of fission product group under consideration, sec,

$t_1 = \text{age of youngest fission}$ products (also equals time since reactor shutdown), sec,

$t_2 =$ age of oldest fission products (also equals time since reactor startup), sec.

For these calculations, it was considered that the power had been constant and equal to 2850 kw, which equals 2700 Btu/sec or $1.78 \times 10^{19}$ Mev/sec. If it is assumed that there is 190 Mev of energy released per fission, then the fission rate, $P$ , is $9.37 \times 10^{16}$ fission/sec. Substitution of these values for $P$ and $K$ in Eq. 1 gives

$$
Q = 1 4. 2 2 \int_ {t _ {1}} ^ {t _ {2}} q (t) d t. \tag {2}
$$

In order to integrate Eq. 2, it is necessary to find a function to fit the curve of $q(t)$ as given in Fig. 92. This was done by dividing the curve into three parts and fitting each of these parts with a different power function. The resulting equations are

$$
\begin{array}{l} q (t) = 0. 7 0 8 t ^ {- 0. 7 2}, (3) \\ q (t) = 6. 4 3 t ^ {- 1 \cdot 1 6}, (4) \\ q (t) = 3. 2 7 t ^ {- 1 \cdot 2} + 9 5. 4 t ^ {- 1 \cdot 4}, (5) \\ \end{array}
$$

(Eq. 5 is the equation recommended in ref. 23 for old fission products).

Equations 3, 4, and 5, were used for integrating Eq. 2 for various values of time after reactor shutdown $(t_{1})$ and for $100\mathrm{hr}$ of reactor operation $(t_{2} = t_{1} + 3.60\times 10^{5})$ . The results are tabulated in the following:

<table><tr><td>TIME AFTER REACTOR SHUTDOWN (sec)</td><td>DECAY ENERGY OF THE FISSION PRODUCTS (Btu/sec)</td><td>TEMPERATURE OF OUTSIDE OF PIPE WALL (°F)</td><td>HEAT LOSS (Btu/sec·lineal ft)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>1150</td><td>0.23</td></tr><tr><td>102</td><td>207</td><td>1500</td><td>0.31</td></tr><tr><td>103</td><td>119</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>104</td><td>58.8</td><td rowspan="3" colspan="2">The temperature drop across the fuel film is given by</td></tr><tr><td>105</td><td>17.7</td></tr><tr><td>3.16 × 105</td><td>4.1</td></tr><tr><td>106</td><td>1.4</td><td rowspan="2" colspan="2">θ = Q/hA,</td></tr><tr><td>107</td><td>0.1</td></tr></table>

These values of decay energy include the energy carried by neutrinos. The energy of the neutrinos is essentially unobservable and, consequently, the actual observable decay energy of the fission products is less than that tabulated above. A rough estimate of the observable energy can be obtained by taking one half the total energy, with the results shown in Fig. 93, where the observable decay energy in the fission products is plotted as a function of the time after reactor shutdown for the conditions of this case (2850 kw, 100 hr, 190 Mev/fission).

# TEMPERATURE DIFFERENCE BETWEEN THERMOCOUPLE ON PIPE WALL AND BULK FLUID

The bulk temperatures of the fluids in the fuel and NaK loops of the ARE are measured by thermocouples welded to the outside of the pipe walls in which the fluid flows. The

$$
\begin{array}{l} 1 \leqslant t <   1 0 0, \\ 1 0 0 \leqslant t <   3. 1 6 \times 1 0 ^ {5}, \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
t > 3. 1 6 \times 1 0 ^ {5}
$$

difference between the measured temperature and the actual fluid bulk temperature has been calculated for the most serious case, that is, the fuel circuit (Fig. 94).

For the configuration shown, the heat loss per lineal foot, as given in chap. 6, is:

The temperature drop across the fuel film is given by

$$
\theta = \frac {Q}{h A},
$$

![](images/6e58d2bd228476c7526bb393555e1c375730f9382e645f29001cd7de70c5ff93.jpg)  
Fig. 93. Disintegration Energy vs. Time after Shutdown.

and the area is given by

$$
A = \pi D = \frac {\pi \times 1 . 0 4 9}{1 2} = 0. 2 7 5 f t ^ {2} / l i n e a l f t,
$$

where

$\theta =$ temperature difference across fuel film, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{F}$

$Q =$ heat loss per lineal foot, Btu/sec·lineal ft,

$h =$ fuel heat transfer coefficient, Btu/sec $\cdot ft^2\cdot \circ F,$

$A =$ heat transfer area per lineal foot, ft²/lineal ft,

$D =$ pipe diameter, ft (1-in., IPS, schedule 40 pipe has an inside diameter of 1.049 in. and an outside diameter of 1.315 in.).

The heat transfer coefficient is given by

$$
h = 0. 0 2 7 \frac {k}{D} \mathrm {R e} ^ {0. 8} \Pr^ {1 / 3} \left(\frac {\mu}{\mu_ {s}}\right) ^ {0. 1 4},
$$

where

$k =$ fuel thermal conductivity, Btu/sec $\cdot ft^2$ (°F/f.t),

Re = Reynolds' number,

$\mathbf{Pr} = \mathbf{Prandtl's}$ number,

$\mu =$ viscosity at bulk temperature, lb/hr·ft,

$\mu_{s} =$ viscosity at surface temperature, lb/hr ft.

For fuel with the properties listed in chap. 1, and a velocity of 6.26 ft/sec,

$$
\mathrm {R e} _ {1 1 5 0} \circ_ {\mathrm {F}} = 1 1, 7 0 0
$$

and

$$
\mathrm {R e} _ {1 5 0 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}} = 2 2, 3 0 0.
$$

If it is assumed that $(\mu / \mu_{*})^{0.14} \simeq 1$

$$
h _ {1 1 5 0} \circ_ {\mathbf {F}} = 0. 9 1 8 \mathrm {B t u} / \sec \cdot \mathrm {f t} ^ {2} \cdot {} ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F},
$$

$$
h _ {1 5 0 0} \circ_ {\mathrm {F}} = 1. 2 4 5 \text {B t u / s e c} \cdot \text {f t} ^ {2} \cdot {} ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F},
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} (h A) _ {1 1 5 0} \circ_ {\mathbf {F}} = 0. 2 5 3 \mathrm {B t u / s e c} ^ {\cdot} \circ F, \\ (h A) _ {1 5 0 0} \circ_ {\mathrm {F}} = 0. 3 4 2 \mathrm {B t u} / \sec \cdot {} ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}, \\ \theta_ {1 1 5 0} \circ_ {\mathbf {F}} = 0. 9 1 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}, \\ \theta_ {1 5 0 0} \circ_ {\mathbf {F}} = 0. 9 1 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}. \\ \end{array}
$$

The temperature drop across the pipe wall is

$$
\theta_ {m} = \frac {Q \ln \frac {D _ {o}}{D}}{2 \pi k _ {m}},
$$

where

$\theta_{\pi} =$ temperature difference across pipe wall, ${}^{\circ}\mathbf{F}$

$D_{o} =$ pipe outside diameter, ft, $k_{m} =$ thermal conductivity of metal, Btu/sec·ft² (°F/ft),

$$
\begin{array}{l} \theta_ {\text {m}, 1 1 5 0} ^ {\circ} = 2. 7 2 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}, \\ \theta_ {\text {m}, 1 5 0 0} ^ {\circ} = 3. 6 7 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {F}. \\ \end{array}
$$

Since the fuel film temperature drop is small and the Reynolds numbers are high, the difference between the fuel bulk temperature (the so-called "mixing cup" temperature) and the fuel maximum temperature will be very small. The total difference between the fuel bulk temperature and the outside pipe wall temperature is

$$
\begin{array}{l} \left(\theta + \theta_ {m}\right) _ {1 1 5 0} ^ {\circ} F = 0. 9 1 + 2. 7 2 = 3. 6 3 ^ {\circ} F, \\ \left(\theta + \theta_ {\pi}\right) _ {1 5 0 0} ^ {\circ} F = 0. 9 1 + 3. 6 7 = 4. 5 8 ^ {\circ} F. \\ \end{array}
$$

At other thermocouple locations in the fuel circuit, the temperature drop in the fuel film is less than the drop calculated above because the above conditions include the minimum insulation thickness and the minimum Reynolds' number encountered in the system. The temperature drop in the film in the NaK circuit is, of course, very small. The temperature drops

UNCLASSIFIED

DWG.A-6-0-25A

![](images/7f070f2ff19270ae8a8d94b694aeaa8bcb1054850c52021d657ed19cb7a2f856.jpg)  
Fig. 94. Thermocouple Assembly.

through the pipe wall at various other thermocouple locations are given in Table 23. The maximum temperature differential between the outside of a pipe and the bulk of the fluid is approximately $4.6^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ . It is important to note that if the flow rates in either the fuel or NaK circuits are reduced, for some unforeseen reason, during operation, the temperature

differential between the outside of the pipe wall and the bulk of the fluid will increase. This increase in temperature differential, since it occurs only within the fluid, should not become serious so long as the flow is turbulent; in the laminar region, however, the temperature differential can become great enough to render the thermocouple unreliable.
